Gregg Mitchell, Mati Kahru, John Wieland, and Malgorzat Stramska, Scripps Institution of Oceanography,\nUniversity of California San Diego\n\nNational Aeronautics and\nSpace Administration\nGoddard Space Flight Center\nGreenbelt, Maryland 20771\n\nMay 2003\n\nAvailable from:\nNASA Center for AeroSpace Information\n7121 Standard Drive\nHanover, MD 21076\xe2\x80\x931320\nPrice Code: A17\n\nNational Technical Information Service\n5285 Port Royal Road\nSpringfield, VA 22161\nPrice Code: A10\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nPreface\nThis document stipulates protocols for measuring bio-optical and radiometric data for the Sensor\nIntercomparison and Merger for Biological and Interdisciplinary Oceanic Studies (SIMBIOS) Project activities and\nalgorithm development. The document is organized into 7 separate volumes as:\nOcean Optics Protocols for Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4\nVolume I: Introduction, Background and Conventions\nVolume II: Instrument Specifications, Characterization and Calibration\nVolume III: Radiometric Measurements and Data Analysis Methods\nVolume IV: Inherent Optical Properties: Instruments, Characterization, Field Measurements and Data\nAnalysis Protocols\nVolume V: Biogeochemical and Bio-Optical Measurements and Data Analysis Methods\nVolume VI: Special Topics in Ocean Optics Protocols\nThe earlier version of Ocean Optics Protocols for Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 3 (Mueller\nand Fargion 2002, Volumes 1 and 2) is entirely superseded by the seven Volumes of Revision 4 listed above.\nThe new multi-volume format for publishing the ocean optics protocols is intended to allow timely future\nrevisions to be made reflecting important evolution of instruments and methods in some areas, without reissuing the\nentire document. Over the years, as existing protocols were revised, or expanded for clarification, and new protocol\ntopics were added, the ocean optics protocol document has grown from 45pp (Mueller and Austin 1992) to 308pp in\nRevision 3 (Mueller and Fargion 2002). This rate of growth continues in Revision 4. The writing and editorial tasks\nneeded to publish each revised version of the protocol manual as a single document has become progressively more\ndifficult as its size increases. Chapters that change but little, must nevertheless be rewritten for each revision to\nreflect relatively minor changes in, e.g., cross-referencing and to maintain self-contained consistency in the protocol\nmanual. More critically, as it grows bigger, the book becomes more difficult to use by its intended audience. A\nmassive new protocol manual is difficult for a reader to peruse thoroughly enough to stay current with and apply\nimportant new material and revisions it may contain. Many people simply find it too time consuming to keep up\nwith changing protocols presented in this format - which may explain why some relatively recent technical reports\nand journal articles cite Mueller and Austin (1995), rather than the then current, more correct protocol document. It\nis hoped that the new format will improve community access to current protocols by stabilizing those volumes and\nchapters that do not change significantly over periods of several years, and introducing most new major revisions as\nnew chapters to be added to an existing volume without revision of its previous contents.\nThe relationships between the Revision 4 chapters of each protocol volume and those of Revision 3 (Mueller\nand Fargion 2002), and the topics new chapters, are briefly summarized below:\nVolume I: This volume covers perspectives on ocean color research and validation (Chapter 1), fundamental\ndefinitions, terminology, relationships and conventions used throughout the protocol document (Chapter 2),\nrequirements for specific in situ observations (Chapter 3), and general protocols for field measurements, metadata,\nlogbooks, sampling strategies, and data archival (Chapter 4). Chapters 1, 2 and 3 of Volume I correspond directly to\nChapters 1, 2 and 3 of Revision 3 with no substantive changes. Two new variables, Particulate Organic Carbon\n(POC) and Particle Size Distribution (PSD) have been added to Tables 3.1 and 3.2 and the related discussion in\nSection 3.4; protocols covering these measurements will be added in a subsequent revision to Volume V (see\nbelow). Chapter 4 of Volume I combines material from Chapter 9 of Revision 3 with a brief summary of SeaBASS\npolicy and archival requirements (detailed SeaBASS information in Chapter 18 and Appendix B of Revision 3 has\nbeen separated from the optics protocols).\nVolume II: The chapters of this volume review instrument performance characteristics required for in situ\nobservations to support validation (Chapter 1), detailed instrument specifications and underlying rationale (Chapter\n2) and protocols for instrument calibration and characterization standards and methods (Chapters 3 through 5).\nChapters 1 through 5 of Volume II correspond directly to Revision 3 chapters 4 through 8, respectively, with only\nminor modifications.\n\ni\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nVolume III: The chapters of this volume briefly review methods used in the field to make the in situ\nradiometric measurements for ocean color validation, together with methods of analyzing the data (Chapter 1),\ndetailed measurement and data analysis protocols for in-water radiometric profiles (Chapter 2), above water\nmeasurements of remote sensing reflectance (Chapter III-3), determinations of exact normalized water-leaving\nradiance (Chapter 4), and atmospheric radiometric measurements to determine aerosol optical thickness and sky\nradiance distributions (Chapter 5). Chapter 1 is adapted from relevant portions of Chapter 9 in Revision 3. Chapter\n2 of Volume III corresponds to Chapter 10 of Revision 3, and Chapters 3 through 5 to Revision 3 Chapters 12\nthrough 14, respectively. Aside from reorganization, there are no changes in the protocols presented in this volume.\nVolume IV: This volume includes a chapter reviewing the scope of inherent optical properties (IOP)\nmeasurements (Chapter 1), followed by 4 chapters giving detailed calibration, measurement and analysis protocols\nfor the beam attenuation coefficient (Chapter 2), the volume absorption coefficient measured in situ (Chapter 3),\nlaboratory measurements of the volume absorption coefficients from discrete filtered seawater samples (Chapter 4),\nand in situ measurements of the volume scattering function, including determinations of the backscattering\ncoefficient (Chapter 5). Chapter 4 of Volume IV is a slightly revised version of Chapter 15 in Revision 3, while the\nremaining chapters of this volume are entirely new contributions to the ocean optics protocols. These new chapters\nmay be significantly revised in the future, given the rapidly developing state-of-the-art in IOP measurement\ninstruments and methods.\nVolume V: The overview chapter (Chapter 1) briefly reviews biogeochemical and bio-optical measurements,\nand points to literature covering methods for measuring these variables; some of the material in this overview is\ndrawn from Chapter 9 of Revision 3. Detailed protocols for HPLC measurement of phytoplankton pigment\nconcentrations are given in Chapter 2, which differs from Chapter 16 of Revision 3 only by its specification of a new\nsolvent program. Chapter 3 gives protocols for Fluorometric measurement of chlorophyll a concentration, and is not\nsignificantly changed from Chapter 17of Revision 3. New chapters covering protocols for measuring, Phycoerythrin\nconcentrations, Particle Size Distribution (PSD) and Particulate Organic Carbon (POC) concentrations are likely\nfuture additions to this volume.\nVolume VI: This volume gathers chapters covering more specialized topics in the ocean optics protocols.\nChapter 1 introduces these special topics in the context of the overall protocols. Chapter 2 is a reformatted, but\notherwise unchanged, version of Chapter 11 in Revision 3 describing specialized protocols used for radiometric\nmeasurements associated with the Marine Optical Buoy (MOBY) ocean color vicarious calibration observatory.\nThe remaining chapters are new in Revision 4 and cover protocols for radiometric and bio-optical measurements\nfrom moored and drifting buoys (Chapter 3), ocean color measurements from aircraft (Chapter 4), and methods and\nresults using LASER sources for stray-light characterization and correction of the MOBY spectrographs (Chapter 5).\nIn the next few years, it is likely that most new additions to the protocols will appear as chapters added to this\nvolume.\nVolume VI: also collects appendices of useful information. Appendix A is an updated version of Appendix A\nin Revision 3 summarizing characteristics of past, present and future satellite ocean color missions. Appendix B is\nthe List of Acronyms used in the report and is an updated version of Appenix C in Revision 3. Similarly, Appendix\nC, the list of Frequently Used Symbols, is an updated version of Appendix D from Rev. 3. The SeaBASS file\nformat information given in Appendix B of Revision 3 has been removed from the protocols and is promulgated\nseparately by the SIMBIOS Project.\nIn the Revision 4 multi-volume format of the ocean optics protocols, Volumes I, II and III are unlikely to\nrequire significant changes for several years. The chapters of Volume IV may require near term revisions to reflect\nthe rapidly evolving state-of-the-art in measurements of inherent optical properties, particularly concerning\ninstruments and methods for measuring the Volume Scattering Function of seawater. It is anticipated that new\nchapters will be also be added to Volumes V and VI in Revision 5 (2003).\nThis technical report is not meant as a substitute for scientific literature. Instead, it will provide a ready and\nresponsive vehicle for the multitude of technical reports issued by an operational Project. The contributions are\npublished as submitted, after only minor editing to correct obvious grammatical or clerical errors.\n\nii\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, 74\n\nv\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nChapter 1\nInherent Optical Property Measurement Concepts: Physical\nPrinciples and Instruments\nScott Pegau1, J. Ronald V. Zaneveld1 and James L. Mueller2\n1\nCollege of Oceanographic and Atmospheric Sciences, Oregon State University, Corvallis\n2\nCenter for Hydro-Optics and Remote Sensing, San Diego State University, California\n\n1.1 INTRODUCTION\nThe Inherent Optical Properties (IOP) of a medium, which describe absorption and scattering interactions that\nmodify a vector light field propagating through it, are defined in Vol. I, Ch. 2, Sect. 2.4 of this protocol document.\nGeometric conventions and notation underlying definitions of, and relationships between IOP and radiative transfer\nin the medium are described in Section 2.2 of Vol. I, Ch. 2. The roles of the IOP in radiative transfer descriptions\nand models of light propagation in the sea are also introduced in Vol. I, Ch. 2, and appear elsewhere in many of the\nVolumes and Chapters of this protocol document.\nThe chapters of this volume (Vol. IV) describe the conceptual background, instrument characteristics, and\nmethods of calibration, field measurements and data analysis for determining the IOP of seawater. The scope of this\nprotocol volume is limited to practical methods for using commercially available instruments1 to determine the IOP\nidentified in Vol. I, Chapter 3 (Table 3.1) of Ocean Optics Protocols for Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation,\nRev. 4. The scope of the present volume does not include applications of measured IOP to aspects of ocean color\nsensor validation that are described elsewhere in the document. The role of IOP in the determination of Exact\nNormalized Water-Leaving Radiance Lex ( \xce\xbb ) as a function of wavelength \xce\xbb, for example, is discussed in Vol. III,\nWN\nChapter 4.\nIn this chapter we extend the IOP definitions and relationships of Vol. I, Ch. 2 to develop the theoretical and\nmathematical bases for practical instrument concepts and methods for measuring IOP in seawater. Instruments will\nbe described to measure, in situ at depth z, the beam attenuation coefficient c ( z , \xce\xbb ) , the volume absorption\ncoefficient a ( z , \xce\xbb ) , and the volume scattering function (VSF) \xce\xb2 ( z , \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) at one or more scattering angles \xcf\x88 .\nSpectrophotometric laboratory methods are also described in detail for determining absorption coefficients from\nfiltered, discrete water samples. Methods of data analysis are described to determine, from combinations of these\nmeasurements, the volume scattering coefficient b ( z , \xce\xbb ) and backscattering coefficient bb ( z , \xce\xbb ) .\nWe do not consider instrument concepts or methods for measuring either fluorescence, or Raman scattering, in\nVol. IV. Even though the Raman and fluorescence cross-sections of water, suspended particles and dissolved\nmaterials are also IOP of seawater, only elastic scattering processes are considered in this volume.\nIn the remainder of Vol. IV, we will ordinarily omit the explicit notation identifying IOP variables as functions\nof depth z in the water column. This is partly a desire for simplifying the notation, but the more important motive is\nto avoid confusing global coordinates (geographic location and vertical depth in the water column) with local\ncoordinates (optical axes and normal planes) used to describe photon-matter interactions and measurement concepts.\n\n1\n\nCertain commercial equipment, instruments, or materials are identified in this chapter to foster understanding.\nSuch identification does not imply recommendation, or endorsement, by the National Aeronautics and Space\nAdministration, nor does it imply that the materials or equipment identified are necessarily the best available for the\npurpose.\n1\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\n1.2 ABSORPTION AND SCATTERING PROPERTIES OF WATER,\nPARTICLES AND DISSOLVED SUBSTANCES\nBecause the IOP are additive (Vol. I, Ch. 2, Sect. 2.4), the absorption and scattering properties of natural water\nconsists of the sums of these IOP of pure water, suspended particles, dissolved substances and turbulence. It is very\ndifficult to experimentally determine the absorption of pure water in the laboratory, due principally to the difficulty\nof making and maintaining pure water during the course of an experiment. Nevertheless, there have been several\nsuccessful experiments (several are cited below), over the past few decades, and the spectral absorption and\nscattering coefficients of pure water are reasonably well known. Using this information, several types of IOP\ninstruments are calibrated by measuring their responses using pure water as an \xe2\x80\x9coptical standard\xe2\x80\x9d. Thus, the\ncalibrated responses of these instruments in field measurements represent the IOP of particles and dissolved\nmaterial, independent of water. IOP.\nFrom another perspective, the general characteristics of absorption and scattering properties vary spectrally, and\nin the case of scattering angularly, between the different optically important constituents of seawater. The contrast\nin angular distribution characteristics of scattering by water and by particles can be an important element of\ninstrument design concepts.\nAbsorption by Pure Water\nThe spectral values recommended in Vol. I, Ch. 2 (Sect. 2.4) for the volume absorption coefficients of pure\n-1\nwater, aw(\xce\xbb) m , are those of Sogandares and Fry (1997) for wavelengths between 340 nm and 380 nm, Pope and\nFry (1997) for wavelengths between 380 nm and 700 nm, and Smith and Baker (1981) for wavelengths between\n700 nm and 800 nm. Here, for wavelengths > 700 nm we recommend values calculated by Van Zee et al. (2002)\nfrom the imaginary part of the refractive indices for pure water measured by Kou et al. (1993). The composite\naw ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum derived from these sources is listed in Table 1.1, together with the linear temperature dependency\n\xe2\x88\x82aw ( \xce\xbb )\n\xe2\x88\x82T\n\n\xef\xa3\xae m -1 \xc2\xb0C \xef\xa3\xb9 reported by Pegau and Zaneveld (1993) and Pegau et al. (1997).\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\nAbsorption by Suspended Particulates and Colored Dissolved Organic Material (CDOM)\nVariations in the spectral absorption of natural waters result directly from variantions in the concentrations and\nchemical compositions of material substances distributed within the water volume. These absorbing materials may\nbe present in seawater either in suspended particulates, such as pigment-bearing phytoplankton, or as solutes (i.e.\nCDOM). Fig. 1.1a illustrates qualitative comparisons between the absorption spectrum of pure water, aw ( \xce\xbb )\n\xe2\x88\x97\n(Table 1.1), a non-dimensional Chl-specific absorption spectrum of phytoplankton pigment concentration, achl ( \xce\xbb )\n\n(Prieur and Sathyendranath 1981), and a typically exponential absorption spectrum of CDOM ag ( \xce\xbb ) (Bricaud et al.\n1981). The amplitude of each absorption spectrum in Fig. 1.1a is arbitrarily scaled to illustrate the characteristic\ndifference in shapes between the constant water background absorption and two varying absorption components\nassociated with Chl and CDOM concentrations. The unique shape and magnitude of the specific absorption\nspectrum for each individual constituent allows measured values of, e.g., Chl and CDOM concentrations to be\ndetermined from measurements of a ( \xce\xbb ) at several appropriate wavelengths. The strong inverse depencence of\nremote sensing reflecance on a ( \xce\xbb ) (Vol. III, Ch. 4), together with the distinctive shape and magnitude\ncharacteristics of the constituents, similarly provides the physical basis for ocean color algorithms for determining\ntheir concentrations from satellite measurements of water leaving radiance at several wavelengths.\nIn Case 1 waters, it is often useful to assume (Gordon and Morel 1983; Morel and Maritorena 2001; Mobley\nand Sundman 2000) that particle absorption ap ( \xce\xbb ) is dominated by phytoplankton pigments and may be expressed\n\xe2\x88\x97\nas a function of Chl concentration [mg m-3] and a Chl-specific absorption spectrum achl ( \xce\xbb ) (Prieur and\n\nSathyendranath 1981), and that CDOM concentration is correlated with Chl so that ag ( \xce\xbb ) may also be calculated as\n\n2\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nFig. 1.1: Spectral variations of absorption in seawater. a: Qualitative comparison of the shapes of\nabsorption spectra of pure water (Table 1.1), specific absorption by Chl (Prieur and Sathyendranath\n1981), and CDOM as implemented in the HYDROLIGHT radiative transfer model (Mobley and\nSundman 2000) described further by Morel and Maritorena (2001). b: Comparisons between aw ( \xce\xbb ) ,\nthe absorption spectrum of pure water, and am ( \xce\xbb, Chl ) ,the sum of absorption by suspended particles and\nCDOM for Chl concentrations of 1, 3 and 10 mg m-3, following Mobley and Sundman (2000). Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nan exponential function of wavelength (Bricaud et al. 1981) scaled as a function of Chl. Fig. 1.1b shows the sum\nam ( \xce\xbb; Chl ) = ap ( \xce\xbb; Chl ) + ag ( \xce\xbb; Chl ) calculated, for Chl = 1, 3 and 10 mg m-3 , using this simple model, as\nimplemented in one of the standard IOP spcification options within the HYDROLIGHT radiative transfer model\n(Mobley and Sundman 2000). The subscript \xe2\x80\x9cm\xe2\x80\x9d indicates that the spectra shown in Fig. 1.1b are those that would\nbe measured by an instrument that was calibrated using pure water as a standard reference medium (Chapter 3). The\nabsorption of pure water aw ( \xce\xbb ) is compared with am ( \xce\xbb; Chl ) in Fig. 1.1b, and the corresponsding total absorption\ncoefficient spectra a ( \xce\xbb; Chl ) = aw ( \xce\xbb ) + am ( \xce\xbb; Chl ) are illustrated in Fig. 1.1c.\n\nThe illustrated examples are\n\nadmittedly an oversimplification, but they are adequate as a basis for considering the nature of IOP components of\nthe signal measured by an absorption meter, or transmissometer, at individual wavelengths.\nScattering by Pure Water\nThe spectral values of the pure water volume scattering coefficient bw ( \xce\xbb ) recommended in Vol. I, Chapter 2\n(Sect. 2.4) are those of Morel (1974), as reported by Smith and Baker (1981). Following Van Zee et al. (2002),\nhowever, we recommend here that preference be given to the bw ( \xce\xbb ) scales of Buiteveld, et al. (1994). Both scales\nare listed in Table 1.1 for comparison. The difference between the two scales is \xe2\x89\xa4 0.0001 m -1 at wavelengths\n> 475 nm, increases to \xe2\x89\xa4 0.0005 m -1 as wavelength decreases to 400 nm, and increases further to \xe2\x89\xa4 0.0014 m -1 at\n340 nm. In no instance does the difference closely approach the 0.005 m -1 measurement uncertainty of beam\nattenuation and absorption meters that are commercially available to date.\nThe angular distribution of the molecular scattering phase function \xce\xb2w ( \xcf\x88 ) , as approximated with equation\n(2.29) [Vol. I, Ch. 2] , is illustrated in Fig. 1.2. The magnitude of \xce\xb2w ( \xcf\x88 ) represents the probability that a photon\nscattering interaction with a water molecule will redirect the photon path direction by an angle \xcf\x88 measured from its\noriginal path. The shape of \xce\xb2w ( \xcf\x88 ) is sometimes referred to as \xe2\x80\x9cisotropic\xe2\x80\x9d in the literature, a characterization that is\ntrue only in that the function is axially symmetric and the probabilities of forward and backward scattering are equal.\n\nFig. 1.2: The dashed curve represent the angular distribution of the scattering phase function for pure water,\ncalculated using the approximate Rayleigh scattering model [equation (2.29) of Vol. I, Ch. 2].\n\n5\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nScattering by Particles\nIn many natural waters, the volume scattering coefficient for particles bp ( \xce\xbb ) is comparable to, or larger than,\nthat of pure water. Moreover, the shape of a particle scattering phase function \xce\xb2w ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) , an example of which is\nillustrated as the solid curve in Fig. 1.3, is strongly peaked - by several orders of magnitude - in the forward\ndirection. The extreme contrast between the angular probability distributions of particulate and molecular scattering\n(the dashed curve in Fig. 1.3) are important factors that must be considered when designing transmissometers and\nscattering meters for use in the sea.\n\nFig. 1.3: The solid curve is an example of a measured scattering phase function for ocean water dominated by\nparticle scattering (Petzold 1972; San Diego Harbor Stn. 2040). The dashed curve is the pure water phase function\nfrom Fig. 1.2, shown here for comparison.\nScattering by Turbulence\nRandom fluctuations in water density, induced by turbulence, act to steer photons through very small angles in\nthe forward direction, and therefore, scattering by turbulence is also strongly peaked, by orders of magnitude, in the\nforward direction. Because turbulent fluctuations are completely random in space and time, the variance of photons\nscattered by turbulence at small angles is much greater than the variance associated with small angle scattering by\nparticles (Bogucki et al. 1998)\n\n1.3 RADIANT FLUX TRANSMISSION MEASUREMENT CONCEPTS\nGeometry and Nomenclature\nIn Fig. 1.4 the origin of a local instrument coordinate system is placed at the exit aperture of a source of\nmonochromatic radiant flux \xce\xa6 o ( \xce\xbb, 0, 0, 0 ) \xef\xa3\xaein \xc2\xb5W nm -1 \xef\xa3\xb9 2 directed as a collimated beam along the positive zm-axis\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\n\xcb\x86 \xcb\x86 \xcb\x86\n(see also Fig. 2.2 in Vol. I, Ch. 2). The subscript \xe2\x80\x9cm\xe2\x80\x9d associated with the coordinate basis vectors ( x m , y m , z m ) in\n\n2\n\nThe choice of these units, rather than, e.g., W m -1 , is customary in ocean color science and is used throughout\nthese protocols.\n6\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nFig. 1.4 indicate that the local \xe2\x80\x9cmeasurement\xe2\x80\x9d coordinate frame is associated with a particular instrument concept, as\ndistinguished from global coordinates defining positions and directions in the extended medium (cf Figs. 2.1 and 2.2\nin Vol. I, Ch. 2). The local instrument coordinate framework is related to global coordinates by a translation and\nrotations that are arbitrary and need not be considered in the present context3.\nThe direction associated with an optical path vector intersecting the transmitted beam axis (zm-axis) is described\nby the angle pair ( \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) , where 0 \xe2\x89\xa4 \xcf\x88 \xe2\x89\xa4 \xcf\x80 is measured from the zm-axis and 0 \xe2\x89\xa4 \xcf\x95 \xe2\x89\xa4 2\xcf\x80 is measured from the\nxm-axis counterclockwise in the xmym-plane. The variable r , with various subscripts, will denote geometric\ndistance along any such optical path4. Directional radiant flux at distance r from a source, or from a scattering\ninteraction site within the transmitted beam, is denoted \xce\xa6 ( \xce\xbb, r , \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) . In Fig. 1.4 for example, radiometric flux\nscattered into direction ( \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) at position xS is denoted as \xce\xa6S ( \xce\xbb, 0, \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) , and the scattered flux transmitted in that\ndirection to Detector 2, at position x D , as \xce\xa6S ( \xce\xbb, rD , \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) . Radiometric flux within the beam transmitted to a point\non the zm-axis at distance r from the source is denoted, \xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, r , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) , where the dot indicates that \xcf\x95 is\nindeterminate when \xcf\x88 = 0 or \xcf\x88 = \xcf\x80 .\n\nIn particular, the flux transmitted from the source to Detector 1 is\n\n\xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rT , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) .\n\nTransmittance and Beam Attenuation\nThe shaded rectangle overlaid on the extended zm-axis in Fig. 1.4 schematically illustrates a cylinder (of cross\nsectional area \xe2\x88\x86s ) representing the collimated beam of radiant flux transmitted from the source to Detector 1. The\ngradient in shading represents the exponential decrease in \xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, r , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) with increasing distance r, as photons\ninteract with the medium and are absorbed and scattered out of the beam. During transmission over a path interval\nfrom r to r + \xe2\x88\x86r , the fraction of radiant flux absorbed in the volume \xe2\x88\x86s\xe2\x88\x86r is spectral absorptance, A ( \xce\xbb ) and the\nfraction of flux scattered out of the beam direction in that volume is spectral scatterance B ( \xce\xbb ) (see Vol. I, Chapter\n2, Sect. 2.4).\nOne could envision superimposing a lightly shaded \xe2\x80\x9ccloud\xe2\x80\x9d on Fig. 1.4 to visualize scattered photons escaping\nfrom the beam in all directions, but this would not indicate the directional nature of the scattering losses. Instead,\nthe scattering process is schematically illustrated in Fig. 1.4 as a mottled, shaded path of photons scattered in a\nparticular direction ( \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) at a single location xS in the beam5. At this on-axis location, xS , similar beams could be\ndrawn in any other direction to visually indicate scattered flux intensity and its subsequent transmittance and\nattenuation in the new direction. The same type of graphic could be drawn anywhere along the optical path, and if\nmany were combined we\xe2\x80\x99d generate the aforementioned \xe2\x80\x9cphoton cloud\xe2\x80\x9d masking any indication of the vector nature\nof the scattered radiant field. Nevertheless, that mental construct is adequate for considering transmission\nmeasurement concepts. If the pathlength rT is short enough that photons initially scattered out of the beam have a\nnegligible chance of undergoing two (or more) additional scattering interactions that could return them to the beam,\nthen it may be assumed that they will not be detected by Detector 1 (Fig. 1.4)6.\n\n3\n\nWhen the IOP are used in the context of a radiative transfer model, on the other hand, the translations and\nrotations relating local coordinates, used to describe scattering interactions (e.g. as at position xS .in Fig. 1.3), to\nglobal coordinates, describing locations and directions in the medium as whole, become critically important.\n4\nSee Footnote 2 in (Vol. 1, Chapter 2) regarding the usage of the variable z in Fig. 2.2 and Sect. 2.4 of that chapter.\nHere we have substituted the symbol r for the optical pathlength along the z-axis in Fig. 1.3 (compare with Fig. 2.2\nof Vol. 1 Ch. 2).\n5\nThe transmission of the illustrated beam of scattered photons to Detector 2 will be further considered as a starting\npoint in the discussion of scattering measurement concepts in Sect. 1.5 below.\n6\nThe question, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat pathlength limit is \xe2\x80\x98short enough\xe2\x80\x99 to avoid multiply scattered photons from reaching a\ntransmissometer\xe2\x80\x99s detector?\xe2\x80\x9d is considered in Chapter 2 of this Protocol Volume.\n7\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nFigure 1.4: The local \xe2\x80\x9cInstrument Coordinate\xe2\x80\x9d framework describing optical beam transmission and scattering\ngeometry. If the source and collector are equal in area and the\nwater path between them (the shaded transmission path volume in Fig. 1.4) were enclosed in a perfectly reflecting\ntube, then all forward scattered photons would be redirected into the beam and reach the detector. For the present,\nwe will postpone consideration of the flux loss due to backscattered photons and treat it as being negligible. Under\nthis construct and assumption, equation (1.3) may be rewritten as\n\xef\xa3\xb1\n\xef\xa3\xbc\n\xef\xa3\xb1\n\xef\xa3\xbc\n\xef\xa3\xb4 \xce\xa6 ( \xce\xbb ) + \xce\xa6 B ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 \xce\xa6i ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb4\n\xef\xa3\xb4 A(\xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb4\nlim \xef\xa3\xb2 T\nlim\n\xef\xa3\xbd = \xe2\x88\x92 \xe2\x88\x86r \xe2\x86\x92 0 \xef\xa3\xb2\n\xef\xa3\xbd,\n\xe2\x88\x86r \xe2\x86\x92 0\n\xce\xa6 i ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x86r\n\xef\xa3\xb4\n\xef\xa3\xb4\n\xef\xa3\xb4\n\xef\xa3\xb4\n\xef\xa3\xb3 \xe2\x88\x86r \xef\xa3\xbe\n\xef\xa3\xb3\n\xef\xa3\xbe\nexpressed in differential form as\nd \xce\xa6 K ( \xce\xbb, r )\n\xce\xa6 ( \xce\xbb, r )\n\n= \xe2\x88\x92a ( \xce\xbb ) dr ,\n\n(1.8)\n\n(1.9)\n\nand integrated over the path from 0 to rT to obtain\na (\xce\xbb) =\n\nln \xce\xa6 o ( \xce\xbb, 0, 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) \xe2\x88\x92 ln \xce\xa6 K ( \xce\xbb, rT , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 )\n\n=\n\n\xe2\x88\x92 ln TK ( \xce\xbb, rT )\n\nm -1 .\n(1.10)\nrT\nrT\nThe reflecting tube method has been used to measure spectral absorption in the laboratory for many decades\n(James and Birge 1938). In recent years, this method has been adapted for use in the ocean (Zaneveld et al. 1992).\nSuitable instruments are now commercially available and are coming into general use within the oceanographic\ncommunity. The best-known, commercially available example of this type of instrument is the \xe2\x80\x9cac-9\xe2\x80\x9d manufactured\nby WET Labs Inc. of Philomath, OR. Protocols for calibrating and using reflecting tube absorption meters, and\nmethods of data analysis are described in Chapter 3 of this volume, and in more specific detail for the ac-9 by Van\nZee et al. (2002), which is available at (www.wetlabs.com).\n\n9\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nLaboratory Methods for Determining Absorption Coefficients\nProtocols in Chapter 4, by Mitchell et al., describe methods for filtering seawater to capture suspended particles\non GF/F filters, and for measuring the absorption spectra of the particle-laden filters with a laboratory\nspectrophotometer. Methods are also described for extracting phytoplankton pigments from the filters, and\nmeasuring the residual absorption spectrum of particulate materials other than phytoplankton pigments. Finally,\nlaboratory methods are also described for measuring the absorption spectrum of CDOM in filtered seawater\nsamples. The material in this chapter derives from the results of recent experimental intercomparison workshops in\nwhich the authors participated, as well as from the published literature. Chapter 4 of this volume is a reformatted,\nbut otherwise unchanged, version of Chapter 15 in Revision 3 to the ocean optics protocols (Mueller and Fargion\n2002).\nAbsorption Determinations from Radiometric Measurements of Irradiance Flux Divergence\nIn situ spectral absorption coefficient profiles can also be measured with spectral radiometers conforming to the\nperformance specifications listed in Vol. II, Chapter 2, if the radiometric package is extended to measure Ed(z,\xce\xbb) and\no\n\no\n\nEu(z,\xce\xbb), as well as scalar irradiances Ed ( z , \xce\xbb ) and Eu ( z , \xce\xbb ) . This combination may be approached either using\nhemispherical collectors to measure upwelling and downwelling hemispherical irradiances (Hojerslev 1975), or by\nusing cosine collectors on one radiometer in tandem with spherical collectors on another radiometer. Given these\nirradiance components, spectral absorption is then computed using Gershun\'s equation (Gershun 1939) as\na ( z, \xce\xbb ) = K ( z, \xce\xbb )\n\nE ( z, \xce\xbb )\no\n\nE ( z, \xce\xbb )\n\n,\n\n(1.11)\n\nwhere E ( z , \xce\xbb ) = Ed ( z , \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 Eu ( z , \xce\xbb ) is vector irradiance, K ( z , \xce\xbb ) is the vertical attenuation coefficient for vector\no\n\no\n\no\n\nirradiance, and scalar irradiance E ( z , \xce\xbb ) = Ed ( z , \xce\xbb ) + Eu ( z , \xce\xbb ) (see also Vol. I, Chapter 2).\no\n\nComparisons between absorption profiles measured using Gershun\'s equation with E ( z , \xce\xbb ) and E ( z , \xce\xbb ) (scalar\nirradiance) data, and absorption profiles measured with a reflecting tube instrument, agreed within 8% (Pegau et al.\n1994). This level of agreement is well within the calibration uncertainties of the particular prototype instruments\nused for that experiment, which were approximately 10% uncertainties in both the scalar irradiance radiometer and\nin the reflecting tube instrument. Less than 5% uncertainties in absorption are expected in future experiments,\nassuming the data are properly averaged to remove near-surface irradiance fluctuations caused by surface waves\n(Zaneveld et al. 2001). In very clear oligotrophic water, moreover, uncertainty in water absorption values may make\nit impossible to realize this level of relative agreement.\nOther Methods of Measuring Absorption\nThere are several other measurement concepts that may be used to determine the absorption coefficient of\nseawater, however, none of them are currently useful for making routine measurements during daylight conditions at\nsea. One possible exception is the determination of absorption by inverting radiance distribution profiles measured\nusing a camera system (Voss 1989), but at present, such camera systems are not commercially available7. In a\nsimilar method, absorption may be determined by inverting the radiative transfer equation for several measured\nmoments of an axially symmetric radiance distribution in the asymptotic regime (Zaneveld and Pak 1972; Wells\n1983) Other examples include the determination aw ( \xce\xbb ) using an integrating cavity (Pope and Fry 1997),\nphotothermal methods (Sogandares and Fry 1997), and differential optoacoustic spectroscopy (Voss and Trees\n1987). Optoacoustic spectroscopy was also used to measure ap ( \xce\xbb ) in phytoplankton cultures (Trees and Voss\n1990). Absorption may also be determined from measurements of irradiance divergence from a submerged isotropic\n7\n\nIt would be advantageous were this situation to change in the future, as such camera systems are potentially even\nmore useful for determining the Bidirectional Reflectance Distribution Function (BRDF) and Exact Normalized\nWater-Leaving Radiance (Vol. III, Ch. 4).\n10\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nsource (Maffione et al. 1993, and earlier references cited there). The present version of this volume does not\naddress any of these methods.\n\n1.5 SCATTERING MEASUREMENT CONCEPTS\nScattering Coefficient Determinations\nThere is no practical way to directly measure the volume scattering coefficient b ( \xce\xbb ) . Given measurements of\nabsorption and beam attenuation coefficients, however, the volume scattering coefficient may computed from (1.1)\nas b ( \xce\xbb ) = c ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 a ( \xce\xbb ) , m -1 . In practice it\xe2\x80\x99s not quite that simple, and several interrelated scattering and absorption\ncorrections must be applied (Chapters 3 and 5 in this Volume). Alternatively, attempts have been made to calculate\nb ( \xce\xbb ) by integrating measurements of the VSF, e.g., by Petzold (1972).\nVolume Scattering Function Measurements\nIn Fig. 1.4, the combination of the source at xo , directional scattering at xS , and Detector 2 at location x D\n\nschematically illustrate the conceptual elements of a scattering meter designed to measure the VSF \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) .\nCollimated flux \xce\xa6 o ( \xce\xbb, 0, 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) is transmitted, in a beam of cross-sectional area A, from the source to xS as\n\xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rS , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) = \xce\xa6 o ( \xce\xbb, 0, 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) e\xe2\x88\x92 c ( \xce\xbb ) rS .\n\n(1.12)\n\nThe irradiance incident normal to the optical transmission axis at xS is\nEi =\n\n\xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rS , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 )\n\n=\n\n\xce\xa6 o ( \xce\xbb, 0, 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) e\xe2\x88\x92 c ( \xce\xbb ) rS\n\n.\n(1.13)\nA\nA\nAt position xS , some of the incident radiant flux is scattered in direction ( \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) , into the solid angle field-of-view\n\xe2\x84\xa6 FOV of Detector 1, from the volume V ( \xcf\x88 ) defined by the intersection of the beam and detector field of view. If that is true, then how can bb ( \xce\xbb ) be determined using the so-called \xe2\x80\x9cbackscattering meters\xe2\x80\x9d in common use\nwithin the community? These instruments actually measure the VSF \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) at one angle \xcf\x88\xe2\x88\x97 , or several angles\n\xcf\x88\xe2\x88\x97 , i = 1, 2,\xe2\x80\xa6 , N , in the backward direction. Ronald V. Zaneveld1 and James L. Mueller2\n1\nCollege of Oceanographic and Atmospheric Sciences, Oregon State University, Corvallis\n2\nCenter for Hydro-Optics and Remote Sensing, San Diego State University, California\n\n2.1 INTRODUCTION\nBeam transmittance T ( \xce\xbb, rT ) over an optical path of length rT m, and the beam attenuation coefficient\nc ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xae m -1 \xef\xa3\xb9 , are introduced in Chapter 1 (Sect. 1.3). The two variables are related by equation (1.6). A beam\n\xef\xa3\xb0 \xef\xa3\xbb\ntransmissometer is an instrument that combines a source of collimated spectral radiant flux \xce\xa6 o ( \xce\xbb, 0, 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) and a co-\n\naligned detector, to measure the flux \xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rT , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) transmitted over distance rT , to measure T ( \xce\xbb, rT ) (Fig. 1.4 and\nrelated text in Sect. 1.3, Ch. 1). A beam transmissometer is also frequently called a beam attenuation meter, or a cmeter.\n\n2.2 TRANSMISSOMETER DESIGN CHARACTERISTICS\nIn concept, a beam transmissometer is a relatively simple instrument to build, and the derived beam attenuation\ncoefficient is needed in many optical studies of the sea. Therefore, instruments of this type have been in use for\nmany years. While a great number of different transmissometer designs have appeared, most follow one of the two\nbasic designs illustrated in Fig. 2.1.\nDirect and Folded Path Transmissometers\nProbably the most common transmissometer design uses a collimated light beam8, with a source in one housing\nand a detector facing the source (Fig. 2.1, top panel). In such an ideal direct-path transmissometer, either a white\nlight, or a light emitting diode (LED), source is combined with a pinhole to provide a point source. A lens is\ninserted into the path to collimate the light beam, an interference filter is inserted to select the waveband of the\nmeasurement, and the light is passed into the water through a window. At the other end of the optical path, the light\nenters the detector assembly through another window and is focused by a lens. An aperture at the focal point\nremoves off-axis scattered light, and the transmitted light falls on the detector. Although this instrument is\nconceptually simple, it is difficult to build. The alignment of components is critical, and something as simple as the\nfilament in the source sagging as the instrument is moved can create significant apparent changes in the derived\nbeam attenuation coefficient.\nSeveral commercial transmissometers9 including, some laboratory\n8\n\nCylindrically limited beam, as opposed to collimated beam, transmissometers will be discussed later in this\nsection.\n9\nCertain commercial equipment, instruments, or materials are identified in this chapter to foster understanding.\nSuch identification does not imply recommendation, or endorsement, by the National Aeronautics and Space\nAdministration, nor does it imply that the materials or equipment identified are necessarily the best available for the\npurpose.\n15\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nspectrophotometers, and the (former) SeaTech and WET Labs field instruments use this basic design. Design\nvariations include the addition of a reference detector and placing the wavelength filter in the detector housing.\nThe folded pathlength design (Fig. 2.1, bottom panel) uses one or more reflectors to create a longer pathlength.\nThe basic idea for this design can be attributed to Petterson (1934). Initial designs used plane mirrors to expand the\npathlength (Wattenberg, 1938; Timofeeva, 1960). The introduction of prisms to separate the incident and reflected\nbeam (Nikolayev and Zhil\xe2\x80\x99tsov 1968; Petzold and Austin 1968) and of concave mirrors as the reflectors, have led to\nimproved versions of this general design. An optical pathlength of 10 m was achieved by Jerlov (1957) by using\nmultiple reflections between three concave mirrors. A currently available commercial instrument with a folded path\nis the HOBILabs c meter.\n\nFig. 2.1 Schematic illustrations of direct path (top panel) and folded path (bottom panel) beam transmissometers\ndesigns.\n\nOther Types of Transmissometers\nA variable pathlength transmissometer is probably the most desirable, and elusive, c-meter design concept.\nOne desirable factor would be such an instrument\xe2\x80\x99s ability to adjust the pathlength to make it optimal for the\nmeasuring conditions (see Pathlength Considerations, below). More importantly, the variable pathlength instrument\nis self-calibrating. To understand this property of such an instrument, examine the basic equation (1.6) for\ntransmissometer measurements. For any transmissometer measurement over pathlength ri , the dark-corrected\ndetector output VT ( \xce\xbb, ri ) is proportional to the flux reaching the detector window \xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, ri , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) .\n\nIf two\n\ntransmissometer measurements are made using different path lengths, r1 and r2 , the transmittance over the\npathlength difference between the two measurements is simply\nT ( \xce\xbb, r2 \xe2\x88\x92 r1 ) \xe2\x89\xa1\n\n\xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, r2 , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 )\n\xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, r1 , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 )\n\n16\n\n=\n\nVT ( \xce\xbb, r2 )\nVT ( \xce\xbb, r2 )\n\n,\n\n(2.1)\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nand c ( \xce\xbb ) may be calculated from (1.6) with rT = r2 \xe2\x88\x92 r1 . The assumptions implicit in this calculation are that the\nbeam attenuation coefficient is constant over the time and space extents of the measurements, and that the optical\nalignment and electronic properties of the instrument also are constant over time.\nBarth et al. (1997) describe the design and application of a variable pathlength instrument for use in coastal\nwaters. However, they also note that the errors in alignment made their instrument unsuitable for clear water\napplications. The requirement to exactly repeat the optical alignment at two distances is the most difficult aspect of\nbuilding a variable pathlength instrument. Small changes in the alignment of the reference detector, or reflector will\nintroduce large errors in the beam attenuation coefficient by causing the focal point of the beam to wander relative to\nthe aperture in front of the detector. If biofouling exists, the spatial gradients in the fouling will cause VT ( \xce\xbb, ri ) to\nvary if the alignment is not perfect. Additionally, if the beam is not truly collimated, but instead has a slight\ndivergence, the beam divergence will cause a different area of the detector window to be illuminated in each\nmeasurement, and any spatial gradients in the optical properties of the window will translate into errors in c ( \xce\xbb ) .\nMany laboratory benchtop spectrophotometers have a design very similar to a collimated beam\ntransmissometer. A complication that arises when using laboratory spectrophotometers to measure beam attenuation\nis that much of the scattered light is kept in the sample by the total-internal-reflection at the glass-air interface. This\nmakes it more likely that multiply scattered light will be received at the detector. This problem can be reduced by\nthe addition of light baffles within the sample cuvette.\nSource and Detector Characteristics\nThe transmittance ratio\n\n\xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rT , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 )\n\xce\xa6 o ( \xce\xbb, 0, 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 )\n\n, i.e. the ratio of the flux transmitted to the detector window divided by\n\nthe flux entering the water at the source window, must be known to compute c ( \xce\xbb ) from (1.6). A transmissometer\ndoes not actually measure either of these quantities. A transmissometer\xe2\x80\x99s detector output signal VD ( \xce\xbb ) represents its\nresponse in the presence of flux \xce\xa6 D ( \xce\xbb ) , the part of \xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rT , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) that arrives at the detector after passing through\nthe instrument\xe2\x80\x99s detector assembly window and other optical elements (Fig. 2.1). Because of reflections and\nabsorption during transmission through windows and other optical components, \xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rT , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) > \xce\xa6 D ( \xce\xbb ) , but\ndark\nassuming the optical throughput is linear, \xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rT ,0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) \xe2\x88\x9d \xce\xa6 D ( \xce\xbb ) . A beam splitter\nbefore the source window can be used to shunt a proportion of the source light to a reference detector to provide a\nmeasure of the flux being sent into the water. It is not\npractical to determine the system response constants based on first principles, because they are dependent on the\noptical component throughputs, the combined response(s) of the detector(s), and electronic circuits. Instead , a\nsystem\xe2\x80\x99s calibration constant CT [dimensionless in (2.4), or in V-1 in (2.5)] is typically determined by measuring the\ninstrument\xe2\x80\x99s output in a \xe2\x80\x9cstandard\xe2\x80\x9d medium having a known beam attenuation coefficient cSTD ( \xce\xbb ) . For\noceanographic transmissometers, the \xe2\x80\x9cstandard\xe2\x80\x9d medium is highly purified water (Sect. 2.3 below), and\ncSTD ( \xce\xbb ) = cw ( \xce\xbb ) (Ch. 1, Sect. 1.2).\nTransmissometer Response Temperature Dependence\nThe source output, responsivity of the detector, and performance of other electronic components tend to be\ntemperature dependent. This causes the calibration constants to be temperature dependent. Two approaches are\nused to remove the temperature dependence, 1) add compensating electronics that allow the voltage output to remain\nconstant over a temperature range, or 2) measure the temperature of the instrument and determine the how the\nconstants change with temperature. The first technique is used in many single-wavelength transmissometers, such\nas the Sea Tech and WET Labs transmissometers. The second approach is used in the WET Labs ac-9 spectral\nabsorption and beam attenuation meter.\nSpectral Characteristics\nMany areas of research in ocean optics require knowledge of the spectral beam attenuation coefficient c ( \xce\xbb ) at\nmore than one wavelength \xce\xbb. Several c-meters have been built to provide this spectral information. Matlack (1974)\nused an instrument with a grating monochromator to measure c ( \xce\xbb ) in the wavelength range from 385 nm to\n565 nm. Using a pair of circular wedge interference filters, Lundgren (1975) was able to measure the beam\nattenuation coefficient at wavelengths between 340 nm and 730 nm. More recent transmissometers that use a\nmonochromator as the detector include the one described by Barth et al. (1997), and the WET Labs Histar. Another\ndesign for obtaining the spectral beam attenuation coefficient utilizes several interference filters mounted in a wheel\nthat rotates them through the beam. Examples of filter-wheel c-meter designs include the VLST (Petzold and Austin\n1968) and the WET Labs ac-9 (Moore et al. 1992; Van Zee et al. 2002).\nBeam Geometry, Detector Acceptance Angle and Scattered Light\nReal transmissometers do not have perfectly collimated sources or detectors. Unlike the idealized detector\nconcept of Fig. 1.4 (Ch. 1, Sect. 1.3), a detector with a finite acceptance angle, or Field of View (FOV) \xcf\x88 FOV ,\n\nM\ndetects photons that are singly scattered in the range 0 < \xcf\x88 \xe2\x89\xa4 \xcf\x88 FOV . Therefore, the flux \xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rT , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) arriving at a\n\n10\n\nA reference detector may be used in a feedback circuit to stabilize an LED source. However, the reference\ndetector signal is not usually included in the instrument\xe2\x80\x99s data output stream in constant source output designs of\nthis type.\n18\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\ntransmissometer\xe2\x80\x99s detector assembly window and subsequently measured (see above) exceeds the true flux directly\ntransmitted along the path direction \xcf\x88 \xe2\x89\xa1 0 according to\nM\n\xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rT , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) = \xce\xa6 T ( \xce\xbb, rT , 0, \xe2\x80\xa2 ) + 2\xcf\x80\n\n\xcf\x88 FOV\n\n\xe2\x88\xab \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb,\xcf\x88,\xcf\x95 ) sin \xcf\x88d \xcf\x88 ,\n\n(2.6)\n\n0\n\nwhere \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) is the volume scattering function (VSF) (Ch. 1, Sect. 1.5).\n\nIn other words, because a\n\ntransmissometer measures a portion of the forward scattered light, its measurement overestimates the transmittance\nT ( \xce\xbb, rT ) and underestimates the beam attenuation coefficient calculated with equation (1.6). The acceptance angle,\nand thus the scattering error, is dependent on the optical elements of the instrument. There is no standard specified\nfor transmissometer acceptance angle, and each manufacturer may use a different one for each particular instrument\ndesign. Therefore, were the transmittance of a homogeneous water volume to be measured a number of perfectly\ncalibrated beam attenuation meters from HOBILabs, WET Labs, or Sea Tech, for example, each instrument model\nwould yield a slightly different c ( \xce\xbb ) , because of its different acceptance angle. These differences also depend on\nthe shape of VSF.\nThese considerations lead to two questions. What is the best detector acceptance angle choice for a\ntransmissometer design? What method should be used to correct the beam attenuation measurements for scattered\nlight acceptance?\nThe first question appears to have a simple answer. The above discussion and equation (2.8) would seem to\nimply that the smaller the acceptance angle, the better the measurement. That may not be correct. One must further\nconsider what is being measured when choosing the acceptance angle (Pegau et al. 1995), and particularly at very\nsmall angles, in the presence of near-forward scattering. Density fluctuations due to natural, or instrument related,\nturbulence steer the beam into random fluctuations and increase the apparent beam attenuation coefficient (Bogucki\net al. 1998) independently from ordinary molecular and particle scattering processes (Ch. 1, Sect. 1.5). How might\nthis phenomenon affect a particular application of the measurement? Were a person interested in inverting the\nspectral beam attenuation coefficient to determine particle properties, they wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t want a beam attenuation meter\nthat is very sensitive to scattering by turbulence. For active LIDAR imaging systems, on the other hand, it may be\nimportant to know the transmittance effects due to very near forward scattering independent of the sources that may\ndominate the scattering process. From another perspective, the angular resolution of radiative transfer models tends\nto be larger than one degree, so fine angular resolution of the volume scattering coefficient and related beam\nattenuation coefficient is not needed for accurate model calculations (Mobley et al, 1993). For many such\ncalculations it is preferable to smooth the highly forward peaked phase function (Fig. 1.3, Ch. 2) and decrease the\nbeam attenuation coefficient accordingly. Gordon (1993) indicates that for irradiance level radiative transfer it is\npossible to completely disregard scattering in the first 15\xc2\xb0, an angle much larger than the acceptance angles of\ntransmissometers. Finally, from an engineering perspective, it is more difficult to build a stable transmissometer\nwith a very small acceptance angle. Based on these considerations, most transmissometers are designed with an\nacceptance angle < 1\xc2\xb0 .\nThe second question has been addressed by several investigators over the years (Gumprecht and Sliepcevich,\n1953; Jones and Wills, 1956; Jerlov, 1957; Duntley, 1963; Voss and Austin, 1993). Voss and Austin (1993)\nexamined the scattering error for both collimated beam and cylindrically limited instruments designs. They found\nthat the percent error increases with increasing acceptance angle and with increasing c ( \xce\xbb ) . The average error for a\n670 nm transmissometer with a 1.0\xc2\xb0 acceptance angle is approximately 19%. However, accurate correction of an\napparent cM ( \xce\xbb ) measured by that instrument would require knowing both the VSF \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) over the range\n0 < \xcf\x88 \xe2\x89\xa4 \xcf\x88 FOV , and the single scattering albedo \xcf\x89o ( \xce\xbb ) =\n\nb (\xce\xbb)\nc (\xce\xbb)\n\n(Vol. I, Ch. 2, Sect. 2.4). To date, very few reliable\n\nmeasurements have been made of \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) at angles less than 1\xc2\xb0. Given the extreme rate of increase in the\nmagnitude of the VSF for particles \xce\xb2p ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) , and for turbulence, as \xcf\x88 \xe2\x86\x92 0 (Ch. 1, Fig. 1.3), any estimate of its\nintegrated value over the range 0 < \xcf\x88 \xe2\x89\xa4 1\xc2\xb0 would be highly uncertain. That uncertainty would transmit directly into\nany c ( \xce\xbb ) correction algorithm attempting to account for the effects of the near-forward VSF.\n\n19\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nThe best approach to dealing with the effects of scattered light in measured beam attenuation coefficients may\nbe that proposed by both Voss and Austin (1993) and Pegau et al. (1995). That is, do not try to apply any scattering\ncorrections to the measured determination of c ( \xce\xbb ) . Simply report the acceptance angle characteristics of the\ntransmissometer used to make the measurements, and leave all considerations of how to handle scattering artifacts to\nthe user of the data. Internal consistency of IOP is obtained by including light scattered up to a certain acceptance\nangle \xcf\x88 FOV in the beam attenuation coefficient, and not including it in the VSF. We may rerwrite (1.1),\nc ( \xce\xbb ) = a ( \xce\xbb ) + b ( \xce\xbb ) , as\n\n\xcf\x88 FOV\n\n\xcf\x88 FOV\n\nc ( \xce\xbb ) = a ( \xce\xbb ) + 2\xcf\x80\n\n\xcf\x80\n\n0\n\n\xe2\x88\xab \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) sin \xcf\x88d \xcf\x88 + 2\xcf\x80 \xe2\x88\xab \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) sin \xcf\x88d \xcf\x88 ,\n\nor,\ncm ( \xce\xbb ) = c ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 2\xcf\x80\n\n\xcf\x88 FOV\n\n\xe2\x88\xab\n\n\xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) sin \xcf\x88d \xcf\x88 = a ( \xce\xbb ) + 2\xcf\x80\n\n\xcf\x80\n\n\xe2\x88\xab \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) sin \xcf\x88d \xcf\x88 = a ( \xce\xbb ) + b ( \xce\xbb )\nm\n\n\xcf\x88 FOV\n\n0\n\nwhere cm ( \xce\xbb ) and bm ( \xce\xbb ) are the measured beam attenuation and volume scattering coefficients, respectively.\nIn another design variant, the beam is cylindrically limited, rather than collimated. In the cylindrically limited\nlight arrangement, the pinhole at the source is imaged on the receiver lens, and the receiver aperture is focused on\nthe source lens. This design illuminates a large volume of water and uses more of the source light. No currently\navailable commercial instruments use the cylindrically limited design, although at one point in history,\ntransmissometers of this type were manufactured by Martek. The Visibility Laboratory Spectral Transmissometer\n(VLST) was a laboratory-built instrument using a cylindrically limited beam in a folded path configuration (Petzold\nand Austin 1968). Several copies of the VLST, built in the late 1970\xe2\x80\x99s, continued in use to measure c ( \xce\xbb ) until\ncirca 1990.\nPathlength Considerations\nOne issue that must be addressed when designing a transmissometer is what the in-water pathlength should be.\nScientifically, it is important to keep the pathlength long enough that the sample volume presents a statistical\naverage of the surrounding water, and short enough that multiply scattered light is not incorporated into the beam.\nIn most ocean waters, multiply scattered light is not in general a problem for the commercially available\ntransmissometers. If scattered light leaves the beam then it will take two additional scattering events to get the light\nback into the beam and redirected towards the detector. The addition of baffles along the light path can nearly\neliminate any possibility of multiply scattered light being detected in ordinary circumstance. In extremely turbid\nwaters, however, the single scattering albedo is very large, and the volume scattering phase function \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95 ) is\nextremely biased in the near forward direction (Fig. 1.3, Ch. 1). Under such conditions, if rT > 3c ( \xce\xbb )\n\n\xe2\x88\x921\n\nm , there is\n\na significant probability that some fraction of scattered photons will undergo 3 or more successive small angle\nscattering events, re-enter the transmission path, and join the flux reaching the detector. The apparent beam\nattenuation coefficient will be artificially reduced if this occurs.\nThere are also engineering concerns associated with the optical pathlength. The path must be short enough that\nlight reaches the detector; it would do no good to have an instrument with a pathlength rT \xe2\x89\x88 10c ( \xce\xbb )\n\n\xe2\x88\x921\n\nm , because\n\nthe transmitted signal would not be detectable. On the other hand, the pathlength must be long enough for\nattenuation to reduce the transmitted flux enough that the difference in incident and transmitted fluxes are large\nenough to be measurable. Longer pathlengths also reduce the relative uncertainty in the measurement of the\npathlength rT .\nA pathlength in the range c ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x89\xa4 rT \xe2\x89\xa4 3c ( \xce\xbb )\n\xe2\x88\x921\n\n\xe2\x88\x921\n\nis generally considered close to optimal. As electronics and\n\nsources have improved, however, instruments with pathlengths rT < c ( \xce\xbb )\nwide range of oceanic conditions.\n\n20\n\n\xe2\x88\x921\n\nm have been shown to work well over a\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nAmbient Light Rejection in Open and Enclosed Path Transmissometers\nThe basic transmissometer designs (Fig. 2.1) do not physically reject all ambient sunlight, which could add to\nthe measured flux. Enclosed path designs that place the optical path within a cell through which the water is\npumped, such as the ac-9, have more physical blocking of ambient light, but are not totally immune to its effects.\nSome scheme must be developed to remove ambient light artifacts. A simple approach is to measure the signal with\nthe source on and with the source off. The ambient signal with the source off is used as the dark reference for\nrelating output signal to transmitted flux. The current generation of instruments use a more sophisticated, but\nsimilar, approach. The light source is rapidly modulated (chopped) and the detector output is phase locked to the\nmodulation frequency, so that the transmitted flux is proportional to the amplitude of the alternating component of\ndetector output. The key underlying assumption is that the natural light field varies slowly and is not part of the\nalternating signal. This approach may have difficulties when the ambient light also varies rapidly, such as with\nindoor lights that have a 60 Hz fluctuation, or near the ocean surface where waves may rapidly modulate the light\nfield. Even with good electronic rejection of ambient light, it is wise to reduce the possible influence of ambient\nlight by using baffles and careful positioning of the instrument.\n\n2.3 CHARACTERIZATION and CALIBRATION OF BEAM\nTRANSMISSOMETERS\nCalibration With Pure Water\nAs explained above, the calibration constant CT for a transmissometer is determined by measuring its response\nto a \xe2\x80\x9cstandard\xe2\x80\x9d medium having a known value of cSTD ( \xce\xbb ) . The optical \xe2\x80\x9cstandard\xe2\x80\x9d medium commonly used to\ncalibrate oceanographic transmissometers and absorption meters (Ch. 3) is pure water, so that\ncSTD ( \xce\xbb ) = cw ( \xce\xbb ) = aw ( \xce\xbb ) + bw ( \xce\xbb ) . The recommended values of aw ( \xce\xbb ) and bw ( \xce\xbb ) are taken from Table 1.2, as\nexplained in Ch. 1, Sect. 1.2.\nPure water of optical calibration grade is freshly prepared by methods described in Chapter 3. This difficult\nstep is critical, because residual traces of particles and/or dissolved organic material introduce serious calibration\noffsets and relative uncertainties between calibrations. The pure water standard is introduced into the optical path\nby one of two methods:\n1.\n\nAn open path transmissometer must be thoroughly cleaned and rinsed in purified water, and then\nimmersed in a test tank containing the pure water standard. Care must be taken to prevent bubbles\nfrom collecting on the instrument\xe2\x80\x99s optical windows. It is ordinarily not practical to carry out this\ncalibration procedure at sea.\n\n2.\n\nTo calibrate an enclosed path instrument, a volume of the pure water standard is pumped through the\nflow-through measurement cell, as described in detail in Chapter 3 for the ac-9, as an example.\nProcedures to assure bubbles do not form within, or be introduced into, the flow-through measurement\ncell (Ch. 3) must be followed carefully. This pure-water calibration procedure can be carried out at\nsea, and it is recommended to do so daily, whenever possible.\n\nIn either case, after allowing suitable time for the instrument to warm up, the instrument signal outputs in\ndark\nresponse to flux transmitted in the pure water standard and dark (ambient) background, VD,w ( \xce\xbb ) and VD,w ( \xce\xbb ) [and\ndark\nif appropriate, also VR,w ( \xce\xbb ) and VR,w ( \xce\xbb ) ], are recorded over a several minute sampling period and averaged.\n\nFor pure water, the forward scattering is sufficiently small that the acceptance angle has little effect on the\ncalibration. From equation (1.7) (Ch. 1), the transmittance of the pure water standard is Tw ( \xce\xbb, rT ) = e \xe2\x88\x92 cw ( \xce\xbb ) rT . This representation and\napproach perhaps simplifies the determination of c ( \xce\xbb ) for the inexperienced user, but at the same time obscures the\nvalue of cw ( \xce\xbb ) used to determine the offset reference voltage.\n\n22\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nAir \xe2\x80\x9cCalibrations\xe2\x80\x9d\nf\ndark,f\nThe sensors output signal response VD,air and dark offset VD,air are recorded in air by the manufacturer at the\n\ntime of each factory water calibration. These values are typically reported with the calibration records, as \xe2\x80\x9cfactory\xe2\x80\x9d\nair calibration and dark values (and thus the superscript \xe2\x80\x9cf\xe2\x80\x9d), to allow the user to periodically record \xe2\x80\x9cair\ncalibration\xe2\x80\x9d, or \xe2\x80\x9cair tracking\xe2\x80\x9d data as a check on instrument stability. Air tracking is primarily intended to be used\nto monitor offsets in the instrument\xe2\x80\x99s output due to changes in the optical system caused by shipping or mounting of\nthe instrument to a cage or other deployment package. Air tracking can also be used to monitor instrument drift over\nextended periods of time. Historically, before the advent of pure water field calibrations, the air calibration was the\nonly stability tracking method available.\nAir tracking data is best obtained in the laboratory, where the environment is consistently clean and dry,\npreferably before and after each transmissometer deployment. Although air calibrations can be performed while in\nthe field, it is at best, difficult to do them on a ship due to the moist environment. Readings in air may be\nsignificantly offset by small amounts of moisture either condensed on, or adsorbed in the windows.\nDetailed protocols for carrying out air calibrations are provided for particular instruments by the manufacturer.\nIn general terms, the protocols include instructions and methods for careful cleaning of optical surfaces, allowing\ntime for exposed optical surfaces to dehydrate in a dry environment, and procedures to avoid or compensate for\ntemperature increases when the instrument is operated in air.\nUser air calibration values can be used to adjust the pure water calibration and responses to correct for\ninstrument drift as\nadjusted\n=\nVD,w\n\ndark\nVD,air \xe2\x88\x92 VD,air\n\nf\ndark,f\n\xef\xa3\xaeVD,w \xe2\x88\x92 VD,w \xef\xa3\xb9 ,\nf\ndark,f\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nVD,air \xe2\x88\x92 VD,air \xef\xa3\xb0\n\n(2.13)\n\nand the adjusted pure water-response and dark values are substituted into (2.10) to calibrate field measurements. If\nthe manufacturer instead provides a factory reference voltage for calibrating the instrument using equation (2.11),\nadjusted\nthe adjusted pure water and dark values should be substituted in (2.12) to determine Vref\n. Air calibration\nadjustments of this type are usually recommended only for instruments with \xe2\x80\x9cconstant\xe2\x80\x9d LED source output, such as\nthe WET Labs C-Star, older SeaTech red transmissometers, and other similar instruments by different\nmanufacturers. Field water calibrations are the recommended basis for correcting drifts in closed path, flow-through\ncell instrument, such as the ac-9.\nInstrument Temperature Dependence\nThe change in a transmissometer\xe2\x80\x99s response and dark values are usually determined by measuring response\nvariations, with the optical path in air, or in a dry, inert gas such as Nitrogen or Argon, as the instrument temperature\nis varied. The response and dark values at each internal instrument temperature (an ancillary measurement and data\noutput needed for temperature corrections) are recorded, and reported either as a lookup table of correction factor\nand temperature pairs, or as the coefficients of a polynomial function of temperature that has been fit to the\ncorrection factors. Instruments that have a closed, flow-through optical cell are usually characterized in a water\nbath, the temperature of which is cycled over a range typically from 5 oC to 30 oC over the course of the experiment;\nto avoid condensation, the flow-through cell is usually filled with a dry, inert gas and sealed. The internal\ninstrument temperatures are somewhat higher than the ambient temperature, due to heating by the electronic circuits\nand source. If this experiment is done with air in the optical path of an open-path transmissometer, i.e. in a\ntemperature controlled chamber, some method must be used and documented to avoid artifacts due to condensation\non the windows.\n\n2.4 FIELD MEASUREMENT METHODS\nThe procedures for measuring in situ profiles, over depth z, of c ( z , \xce\xbb ) using constant output LED source\ntransmissometers are straightforward. The instrument is connected into a data acquisition system and mounted on a\nprofiling cage following the manufacturer\xe2\x80\x99s instructions. If the instrument has an analog output, the user must\n\n23\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nensure that the external analog-to-digital converter used to digitize the readings is calibrated in absolute units of V,\nsince that is the basis on which the instrument has been calibrated.\nThe windows on the beam transmissometer must be cleaned with lens cleaner, or a mild detergent solution, and\na soft cloth, or tissue, rinsed with distilled water, then rinsed with isopropyl alcohol and wiped dry. An approximate\nair calibration reading should be made before every cast to verify that the windows are clean. A transmissometer\ndark voltage should also be measured at this time. These on-deck air calibrations should be logged and compared to\nthe more careful air calibrations done under dry laboratory conditions before and after each cruise (Section 2.3). If\npre- and post-cruise air calibrations are significantly different, the time history should indicate whether the change\noccurred suddenly (e.g. a scratch in the window), or as a drift over time.\nEach time an open-path transmissometer is placed into the water, care must be taken to assure that bubbles do\nnot collect on the windows, particularly if the instrument is mounted in a vertical orientation.\nProtocols covering methods for making field measurements with the ac-9 instrument are described in detail in\nZee et al. (2002). Some critical aspects of these protocols are briefly reviewed in Chapter 3 to emphasize their\nimportance.\n\n2.5 DATA ANALYSIS METHODS\nThere are several generic steps needed to process and analyze a vertical profile of measured transmissometer\ndata:\n1.\n\nMerge the transmissometer data with externally measured depth and temperature data. Assuming that the\ntransmissometer does not have an internal, high-quality depth transducer, it is usually mounted together\nwith a CTD to provide the depth and water temperature fields. If the transmissometer output data record\ndoes not include the internal instrument temperature measured by a built-in thermistor, external water\ntemperature provides the basis for any temperature compensation adjustments that may be required.\n\n2.\n\nApply lag corrections to account for the time interval between when water enters the intake port and when\nit enters the beam-attenuation measurement optical path in a flow-through transmissometer.\n\n3.\n\nSubtract the depth offset between the pressure transducer used to measure package depth and either\n\na.\n\nthe intake port of a flow-through transmissometer, or\n\nb.\n\nthe midpoint of the optical path in an open path transmissometer.\n\n4.\n\nField calibration adjustments should be applied by the methods specified by the manufacturer of a\nparticular instrument. In many cases this will involve entering the changes in an instrument calibration file\nused by the computer software that implements and applies (2.9) or (2.10) to calibrate the data.\na.\n\nPure water calibration results are the preferred source of these adjustments for flow-through\ninstruments.\n\nb.\n\nAir calibration for tracking drift corrections should be applied using only data from calibrations\ncarried out under dry laboratory conditions and showing insignificant variations between replicated\ncalibrations. When the manufacturer represents the calibration coefficients in terms of a reference\nsignal to be applied using (2.11), the corrected air calibration factor is computed using (2.13).\n\n5.\n\nInstrument Internal Temperature Compensation factors should be applied in the manner specified by the\nmanufacturer of a particular instrument.\n\n6.\n\nCompute transmittances T ( \xce\xbb, rT ) , and beam attenuation coefficients c ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 cw ( \xce\xbb ) offsets, relative to\npure water using the appropriate combination of equations (2.9), (2.10), or (2.11) with (1.6) for the\ninstrument type and output data.\n\n7.\n\nAdd pure water cw ( \xce\xbb ) , determined from Table 1.1, to c ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 cw ( \xce\xbb ) to obtain the total beam attenuation\ncoefficient c ( \xce\xbb ) .\n\n24\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nDetailed procedures required to carry out each of the above steps for particular instrument are typically provided by\nthe manufacturer. WET Labs Inc., for example, provides both a User\xe2\x80\x99s Manual for it\xe2\x80\x99s ac-9 absorption and beam\nattenuation coefficient meter, and a detailed ac-9 Protocol Manual (Van Zee et al. 2002); additional information\nfrom the latter document, regarding absorption and beam attenuation measurements, is outlined in Chapter 3.\nMany of the steps listed above apply also when a transmissometer is installed and operated on a ship as a\ncomponent of an along-track measurement system. The lengthy plumbing path in such a system introduces\nintake-to-measurement lags of up to several minutes, while a research vessel typically advances approximately one\nKm in 3 min. Therefore, accurate temporal and spatial co-registration of, e.g., surface water temperature,\nchlorophyll a fluorescence, and c ( \xce\xbb ) requires accurate determination of the flow rate and lag time between a water\nvolume\xe2\x80\x99s intake (usually in a ship\xe2\x80\x99s sea chest), passage through some debubbler apparatus, and its arrival in the\nmeasurement cell of each instrument.\n\nREFERENCES\nBarth, H., K.
