Mueller2\nCollege of Oceanographic and Atmospheric Sciences, Oregon State University, Corvallis\n2\nCenter for Hydro-Optics and Remote Sensing, San Diego State University, California\n\n3.1 INTRODUCTION\nConcepts and methods for measuring the absorption coefficient a ( \xce\xbb ) of seawater are briefly reviewed in\nChapter 1 (Sect. 1.4) of this Volume. Chapter 4 of this Volume is devoted to laboratory spectrophotometric methods\nof measuring absorption of particles and dissolved materials in filtered water samples. The present chapter focuses\non commercially available instruments11 that may be used from ships and moored platforms to practically measure\na ( \xce\xbb ) in support of satellite validation activities. This first version of absorption protocols is particularly focused on\ninstruments that fall under the \xe2\x80\x9creflective tube\xe2\x80\x9d design concept briefly introduced in Sect. 1.4. However, the\nconceptual basis for determining absorption by measuring flux reflected from a diffuse target is also described later\nin this section. Expanded reviews of protocols using instruments based on other design concepts are deferred for\npossible consideration in future revisions to this volume.\nReflective Tube Absorption Meter Concepts\nIn Sect. 1.4 it was observed that to determine the absorption coefficient associated with transmission over an\noptical pathlength rT (Fig. 1.4), it would be necessary to measure the sum of transmitted and scattered flux at the\n\ndetector, \xce\xa6 K ( rT ) = \xce\xa6 T ( rT ) + \xce\xa6 B ( rT ) . Neglecting backscattering, it was suggested that perhaps one might redirect\n\nall forward scattered flux to the detector using an ideal reflective tube, and determine the absorption coefficient as\na=\n\n\xe2\x88\x921 \xef\xa3\xab \xce\xa6 T ( rT ) + \xce\xa6 B ( rT ) \xef\xa3\xb6\nln \xef\xa3\xac\n\xef\xa3\xb7.\n\xef\xa3\xb7\n\xce\xa6o ( 0)\nrT \xef\xa3\xac\n\xef\xa3\xad\n\xef\xa3\xb8\n\n(3.1)\n\nOf course a perfectly reflecting tube cannot be realized in a real instrument. Nevertheless, because the scattering\nphase function of suspended particles in natural waters is strongly peaked in the forward direction (Fig. 1.3), it is\npossible to use this approach to retain more than approximately 85% of scattered photons in the beam reaching the\ndetector of such an instrument. James and Birge (1938) built a laboratory version of such an instrument to measure\nabsorption spectra of lake waters, and Zaneveld et al. (1992) introduced an instrument of this type for in situ\nabsorption measurements. In essence, such an instrument is simply a poor transmissometer (Chapter 2) that fails to\nexclude all of the singly scattered photons from its beam transmittance measurement, and therefore, in its ideal\nrealization would measure only losses due by absorption as per equations (1.9) and (1.10).\nThe transmittance, absorption, scattering and reflection interaction processes that occur in a real reflective tube\nabsorption meter are illustrated schematically in Fig. 3.1. A source emits collimated flux with a cross sectional area\nslightly less that that of the reflective tube, and flux reaching the other end of the tube is measured by a detector that\ncovers its entire cross-sectional area. Ray paths extending directly from the source to the detector indicate direct\ntransmittance of flux. Ray paths that terminate within the water volume enclosed by the tube indicate absorbed flux.\n11\n\nCertain commercial equipment, instruments, or materials are identified in this chapter to foster understanding.\nSuch identification does not imply recommendation, or endorsement, by the National Aeronautics and Space\nAdministration, nor does it imply that the materials or equipment identified are necessarily the best available for the\npurpose.\n27\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nIn natural waters a large fraction of scattered photons are only slightly deflected in the near forward direction (Fig.\n1.3) and proceed directly to the large-area detector without encountering the tube walls. Ray paths with scattering\nangles large enough to encounter the water-quartz interface, where refraction and reflection take place; the refracted\nportion is transmitted to the outer quartz-air interface, where another refraction and reflection interaction occurs.\nFor simplicity in this conceptual discussion, we do not consider multiple reflection and refractive transmittance\ninteractions within the thin quartz layer. Ray paths containing a scattering angle less than the critical angle \xcf\x88 c\nassociated with total internal reflection at the outer quartz-air interface, are totally reflected on each encounter with\nthe tube wall and are transmitted to the detector over a slightly elongated path; for a quartz reflective tube,\n\xcf\x88 c \xe2\x89\x85 42\xc2\xb0 , and thus the total internal reflectance represents a very large fraction of all flux scattered by particles\n\xcf\x80\nundergoes partial\n2\ntransmittance losses [1 \xe2\x88\x92 \xcf\x81g ( \xcf\x88 )] at each encounter with the reflectance tube, with the reflected portion continuing\n\n(Fig. 1.3). Flux transmitted along ray paths with a scattering angle in the range \xcf\x88 c < \xcf\x88 <\n\nover a zig-zag path until either reaching the detector or disappearing due to attenuation by absorption and\ntransmission losses in multiple encounters with the tube wall. Flux along ray paths containing a scattering angle\n\xcf\x80\n\xcf\x88 \xe2\x89\xa5 , i.e. backscattered flux, is lost from the forward transmittance altogether. Backscattering accounts for only a\n2\nfew percent of scattering by marine particulates (Fig. 1.3).\n\nFigure 3.1: Schematic illustration of light interactions and transmission in a reflective tube absorption\nmeter. Ray paths ending in the water represent absorption, and those extending directly from the\nsource to detector represent beam transmittance. Other ray paths indicate scattering interactions: 1)\nbackward scattered paths do not reach the detector, 2) paths with forward scattering at an angle less\nthan the critical angle, i.e. \xcf\x88 \xe2\x89\xa4 \xcf\x88 c , experience total internal reflection by the tube and reach the\ndetector over an elongated optical path, and 3) forward scattered ray paths at angles in the range\n\xcf\x80\n\xcf\x88 c < \xcf\x88 < experience partial losses from the tube at the quartz-air interface, and may or may not\n2\nreach the detector depending on whether the internally reflected path survives the absorption process.\nIn the single scattering approximation, the flux measured by the detector of a reflective tube absorption meter\nmay be written\nrT \xcf\x88 c\n\n\xce\xa6 m ( rT ) = \xce\xa6 T ( rT ) + 2\xcf\x80\xce\xa6 o ( 0 ) \xe2\x88\xab \xe2\x88\xab \xce\xb2 ( \xcf\x88 )e e\n\xe2\x88\x92 cr\n\n\xe2\x88\x92a\n\nrT \xe2\x88\x92 r\ncos \xcf\x88\n\n\xe2\x88\x92a\n\nrT \xe2\x88\x92 r\ncos \xcf\x88\n\nsin \xcf\x88d \xcf\x88dr +\n\n0 0\n\nrT \xcf\x88 c\n\n2\xcf\x80\xce\xa6 o ( 0 ) \xe2\x88\xab \xe2\x88\xab \xce\xb2 ( \xcf\x88 )e \xe2\x88\x92 cr e\n0 0\n\n,\n\xef\xa3\xae\xcf\x81 g ( \xcf\x88 ) \xef\xa3\xb9\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\nN ( rT \xe2\x88\x92 r )\n\n(3.2)\n\nsin \xcf\x88d \xcf\x88dr\n\nwhere \xcf\x81g ( \xcf\x88 ) is net reflectance of the quartz tube beyond the critical angle, and the exponent N (rT \xe2\x88\x92 r ; \xcf\x88) is the\naverage number of wall reflections required for a ray path to reach the detector following a scattering event at\n\n28\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\ndistance r and angle \xcf\x88 . In other words, the weighting coefficient\nW ( \xcf\x88 ) may be interpreted as the fraction of light that is scattered at angle \xcf\x88 that does not reach the absorption\ndetector; it may take values from 0, indicating all light scattered at that angle reaches the detector, to 1, indicating\nthat none of the light reaches the detector. The uncertainty of absorption coefficients determined from\nmeasurements with a reflective tube instrument is largely determined by the uncertainty of the methods used to\ncorrect for the integrated scattering error (Zaneveld et al. 1994), which will be briefly summarized below in Sect.\n3.4. These considerations must be deferred to a possible future revision to this protocol\nvolume.\n\n3.2 CHARACTERIZATION and CALIBRATION OF REFLECTIVE TUBE\nSPECTRAL ABSORPTION METERS\nPerhaps the best-known version of a reflective tube absorption meter is the ac-9 manufactured by WET Labs,\nInc. This instrument measures both the spectral beam attenuation coefficient c ( \xce\xbb ) in an enclosed flow-through\nnon-reflective optical path, and the spectral volume absorption coefficient a ( \xce\xbb ) using parallel enclosed flowthrough reflective tube optical paths, one of which (the absorption side) is a reflective tube (Fig. 3.1). Many aspects\nof the characterization, calibration, field measurements and data analysis protocols relating to this type of instrument\nwere introduced in Chapter 2 and will not be repeated here. Moreover the manufacturer provides extremely detailed\n\n31\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nprotocols for calibrating and using this instrument, and for analyzing its data, both in the ac-9 User Manual, and in a\ndetailed protocol manual (Van Zee et al. 2002), both of which are available online (www.wetlabs.com). Additional\nbackground information related to characterization, calibration and data analysis methods for this instrument may be\nfound in Moore et al. (1992), Zaneveld et al. (1992) and Twardowski et al. (1999). Here we will briefly highlight\ncritical aspects of the protocols that must be carefully followed to obtain accurate a ( \xce\xbb ) measurements using this, or\na similar, instrument in the field. Many of these topics, as they relate to the beam transmissometer side of the\ninstrument, have been discussed in Chapter 2 of this volume.\n\nFig. 3.3 Schematic illustration of the ac-9 beam attenuation and absorption meter\n(courtesy of WET Labs , Inc).\n\nPure Water Calibration\nThe procedure for using pure water to calibrate the reflective tube absorption meter side of an ac-9 is identical\nto that described in Chapter 2 (Sect. 2.4) for its flow-through beam-transmissometer side. The calibration equation\nfor measured absorption am relative to pure water, corresponding to equation (2.9) for c , is\ndark\ndark\n\xef\xa3\xb1\n\xef\xa3\xbc\n1 \xef\xa3\xb4 \xef\xa3\xaeVR,w ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 VR,w ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9 \xef\xa3\xaeVD ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 VD ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9 \xef\xa3\xb4\n\xef\xa3\xbb\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb ,\nln \xef\xa3\xb2 \xef\xa3\xb0\n(3.11)\n\xef\xa3\xbd\ndark\ndark\nrT \xef\xa3\xb4 \xef\xa3\xaeVD,w ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 VD,w ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9 \xef\xa3\xaeVR ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 VR ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9 \xef\xa3\xb4\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\xef\xa3\xbe\n\xef\xa3\xb3\nwhere the signal notations on the right-hand-side are the same as those defined in Chapter 2, and pure water\nabsorption values are listed in Table 1.1 of Chapter 1.\n\nam ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 aw ( \xce\xbb ) =\n\nPure Water Preparation\nThe methods for preparation of optical calibration grade pure water were not addressed in Chapter 2, and are\nincluded here.\n\n32\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nTo prepare pure water for instrument calibrations, the manufacture uses a commercial de-ionization system and\nfiltration system. After primary de-ionization, the water is processed using a Barnstead, or equivalent, purification\nunit and stored in a large holding tank. To maintain purity, water in the holding tank should be re-circulated through\na ultra-violet chamber and additional purification filters. Water for calibration is drawn through a 0.01-micron ultrafilter at the point of delivery. The circulating holding tank allows the highly reactive de-ionized water to equilibrate\nwith the ambient conditions and the ultra-violet chamber prevents any biological contamination from entering the\nreservoir.\nFor field calibrations, one approach is to either purchase HPLC grade pure water, or produce it in the lab, and\ntransport it to the ship, especially for short cruises. On some research vessels, a water deionization and purification\nsystem is permanently installed to support the scientific party. If so, care must be taken to insure that the filters are\nfresh and do not produce Colored Dissolved Organic Matter (CDOM) from decaying particles trapped in the filter.\nAlternatively, a portable system consisting of a commercial filtration unit, such as the Barnstead E-Pure or the MilliQ Q-Pak treatment systems may be set up temporarily on the ship. The input water should be pre-filtered using a\n1.5 \xc2\xb5m commercial filter cartridge and an activated charcoal filter to increase the lifetime of the primary unit. Pure\nwater should be produced in advance of the calibration and stored in a clean 20-liter polycarbonate carboy and be\nallowed to stand for approximately 12 hours to equilibrate with the ambient temperature and to remove bubbles.\nTo calibrate an ac-9, the carboy may be equipped with a cap having barb fittings to connect tubing to a\npressurization unit that pushes water to the instrument (Fig. 3.4). The carboy is pressurized to approximately 10 psi\nusing an oil free air pump, or a tank of dry nitrogen gas. The air supply tube inside the carboy should be kept above\nthe water level to prevent the creation of bubbles when pressurizing the carboy, and the outlet tube should extend\nnearly to the bottom of the carboy. To connect the carboy to the ac-9, Teflon tubing is recommended, rather than\nTygon tubing, which may contain plasticizers that can contaminate the water. The tubing from the carboy is\nconnected to the bottom nozzle on the ac-9 flow tube. The tubing near the ac-9 inlet and outlet should be covered\nwith black tape to avoid ambient light leaks into the optical path. A short piece of tubing with a valve is connected to\nthe top nozzle on the flow tube, both to control water flow through the system, and to provide backpressure, which\nhelps to keep gases in solution and prevents the formation of micro-bubbles. An optional 0.2-micron filter may be\nplaced at the point of delivery.\n\nFig. 3.4. Schematic illustration of a pure-water supply system for field\ncalibrations of an ac-9.\n\nAir \xe2\x80\x9cCalibrations\xe2\x80\x9d\nThis procedure is discussed adequately in Chapter 2.\n\n33\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\n3.3 MEASURING SPECTRAL ABSORPTION COEFFICIENTS WITH\nREFLECTIVE TUBE METERS\nThe ac-9 should be mounted in a profiling instrument cage following directions provided by the manufacturer\n(Van Zee et al. 2002). It is strongly recommended to mount a CTD data the same profiling package as an ac-9, and\nto develop a method for accurately merging the measurements from both instruments; combining the data streams\nfrom the two instruments in real time as part of the data acquisition system is the preferred approach, with a second\nchoice being to time-tag both data records and merge the data on that basis. The basis of these recommendations is\nthat water temperature and salinity data are essential for the corrections described below in Sect. 3.4.\nTo begin a cast, place the instrument package in the water before they are powered up. Then hold the package\nunderwater near the surface allow approximately 5 min for the instruments to warm up, stabilize the electronics, and\nreduce the possibility that thermal shock may adversely affect the measurements. Monitor the instrument outputs\nand wait to begin profiling until the instrument stabilizes at the surface. If an absorption meter will not stabilize its\nreadings after 5-10 minutes under these conditions, it is recommended that it be returned to the manufacturer for\ncharacterization of the problem and necessary repairs.\nIf possible place the instrument 10 to 20 meters below the surface to help purge bubbles. Purging bubbles is\nmore difficult when making filtered measurements (see below). If you are not making filtered measurements the\ninstruments will generally purge at the surface, but purging them below 10 m is still a good idea.\nThe reader is referred to Van Zee et al. (2002) for detailed recommendations concerning rates of descent during\nprofiles using an ac-9.\nAfter the instruments are brought on deck following a cast, they should be immediately rinsed, and the flow\ntubes flushed, with fresh water to mitigate corrosion. If the instrument will be on deck for more than 30 min\nbetween casts, the optics and flow tubes should be cleaned with Nannopure water and dried. At least once per day,\nwhile at sea, the optics and flow tubes should be cleaned with methanol, cleaned again using a mild detergent\ndissolved in Nannopure water, rinsed with Nannopure water, cleaned again with methanol, and finally dried. Use\nsoft tissues, such as KimWipes, to gently clean and dry the optical surface, and be extremely careful to wipe them in\na constant direction and to not scrub the optical surfaces.\nWhen the instrument package is be stored on deck for a prolonged period between casts, cover it with a\ntarpaulin to protect the absorption meter from direct exposure to the sun. Excessive solar heating of the instrument\no\no\nmay exceed the practical limits (5 C to 30 C) of internal temperature corrections for an ac-9, and thus invalidate its\nmeasurements until it has cooled sufficiently to restore normal operations.\nFiltering the Water Intake Port of an ac-9 for Measurements of Absorption by CDOM and Particles\nThe absorption coefficient of dissolved material may be measured by attaching a 0.2 \xc2\xb5m pore-size filter to the\nintake of an ac-9. The recommended practice is to locate the intake filter below the instrument at the bottom of the\ncage. Measurements in the filtered intake configuration are also very useful for testing the operational performance\nof ac-9; quality control procedures using dissolved measurements are discussed in Sect. 3.5.\nExamples of suitable filters are the Gelman Suporcap 100 and Gelman Maxi-cap (0.2 \xc2\xb5m) filters. These filters\nhave high flow rates at low differential pressure and don\xe2\x80\x99t adsorb or leach materials. The outer housings of these\ncommercial filter cartridges may be cut off to allow some flushing of the filter and increase the flow-rate. Make\nsure you don\xe2\x80\x99t handle the filter material, or lay a filter on the deck, where it can be exposed to oil and grease. Hose\nclamp the filter to the tubing and make sure any vents on the filter are closed. Before use, a filter should be either\nflushed for several minutes with DI water, or soaked several hours in DI water, to remove air pockets in the filter\nmembrane. As a note of caution, never flush a filter in the reverse direction!\nThere are alternative ways to plumb the filters into the ac-9 instrument. The preferred arrangement is to filter\nthe a and c sides separately using two filters and two pumps, so that each side is plumbed independently. This\napproach is expensive, however, and is not necessary for measuring the absorption by the dissolved component. For\nmeasurement of the absorption by dissolved materials, only one side needs to be filtered, because scattering by\nparticles less than 0.2 \xc2\xb5m in size is not detectable by an ac-9, and hence the filtered a and c measurements are equal.\nOn the other hand, measurements with filters on intakes of both sides are useful for quality control tests (Sect. 3.5).\n\n34\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nIf possible replace the filter daily. If you choose to replace a filter less frequently, soak it in DI water during\nlong breaks between profile measurements during a deployment. Record the date of each filter change in the cruise\nlog.\nMixing of water within the filter cartridge will smear measurements of a vertical gradient in the absorption by\ndissolved materials, and an unfiltered instrument will detect a gradient in total absorption with better vertical\nresolution. In addition, the reduced flow rate through the filter will increase the time lag to approximately 4 to 6 sec,\ncompared to 1.2 to 1.8 sec for unfiltered measurements. Moreover, the lag rate will gradually increase as a filter\naccumulates particles during its use. The preferred means of determining flow rate and lag corrections is to attach a\nflow-meter in-line into the supply or exhaust tubing. An indirect technique for estimating flow-rate related lag times\nis to match depths of changes in the a(715) channel with depths of strong changes in water temperature; these\nchanges are linked because absorption by water is temperature dependent in the near infrared (Sect. 3.4), and the\ntime lag between matched changes can be derived from the depth separation and the profiler\xe2\x80\x99s rate of descent.\nA combination of unfiltered and filtered ac-9 measurements can be used to derive particle absorption and\nattenuation coefficients, as well as absorption by CDOM. There are several combinations that can be used:\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFrom measurements using a single ac-9 with the c side filtered and the a side unfiltered, particle absorption\ncan be obtained as ap ( \xce\xbb ) = a ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 aw ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 \xef\xa3\xae cg ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 cw ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9 , where the measurements have been corrected\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nusing the methods described in 3.6 below. The subscript \xe2\x80\x9cg\xe2\x80\x9d is associated with CDOM, based on historical\nuse of the term \xe2\x80\x9cgelbstoffe\xe2\x80\x9d, or yellow-matter, as a pseudonym of CDOM.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFrom measurements with two ac-9\xe2\x80\x99s, one filtered and one unfiltered, ag ( \xce\xbb ) is derived directly from the\nfiltered measurements, after the corrections of Sect. 3.6, and ap ( \xce\xbb ) = a ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 ag ( \xce\xbb ) , where a ( \xce\xbb ) is derived\nfrom the unfiltered instrument and the pure water terms cancel. This approach may also be used with a\nsingle instrument by making successive casts, one with the filter attached and the second with the filter\nremoved.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAnother alternative approach is to make successive casts with one instrument, filtering the a intake on one\ncast, and the c intake on the other. The filtered and unfiltered measurements from the two casts are\ncombined as above.\n\nThe approach yielding the lowest instrumental uncertainty of the particulate absorption is to derive it from\nfiltered and unfiltered measurements with the same instrument on successive casts. Calibration offsets, whether\nknown or not, are identical in the filtered and unfiltered measurements on each side, and therefore, the offsets cancel\nwhen particle absorption is determined as the difference between the two measurements. On the other hand,\npotentially larger uncertainty may result from possible changes in the IOP profiles between casts, due to horizontal\nadvection and/or vertical displacement of IOP features by internal waves.\n\n3.6 DATA ANALYSIS METHODS\nThe initial steps in processing absorption measurements using an ac-9 reflective tube absorption meter are\nidentical to those presented for processing beam transmissometer measurements in Chapter 2, Sect. 2.5 [substituting\nequation (3.11) for (2.9) in Step 4). This information will not be repeated here. Two additional analysis steps are\nnecessary to obtain accurate absorption coefficients from combined ac-9, or similar instrument, measurements of\nam ( \xce\xbb ) and cm ( \xce\xbb ) : 1) corrections for water temperature and salinity induced offsets in water absorption and\nattenuation, and 2) corrections for scattering errors [equation (3.3)] in am ( \xce\xbb ) .\nTemperature and Salinity Corrections\no\n\nThe absorption of pure water is dependent on water temperature T [ C] (Pegau and Zaneveld 1993) and the\nabsorption coefficient of seawater is also dependent on its salinity S [PSU] (Pegau et al. 1997). These variations\naffect the measured coefficients of absorption am ( \xce\xbb ) and attenuation cm ( \xce\xbb ) in the following ways:\n\n35\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\n1.\n\nThe difference\n\n(T \xe2\x88\x92 Tr )\n\nbetween the water temperature T during a measurement at sea and the\n\ntemperature Tr of the pure water reference standard at the time the instrument was calibrated changes\nthe water absorption \xe2\x80\x9cbaseline\xe2\x80\x9d value aw ( \xce\xbb ) . This change affects am ( \xce\xbb ) and cm ( \xce\xbb ) equally,\nbecause scattering by pure water is not significantly temperature dependent.\n2.\n\nThe absorption of seawater varies with salinity S, and of course S = 0 PSU for the pure water\nreference standard used to calibrate the ac-9. This additional shift in the water-absorption \xe2\x80\x9cbaseline\xe2\x80\x9d\nalso affects am ( \xce\xbb ) and cm ( \xce\xbb ) equally.\n\n3.\n\nThe salinity dependent variations in the refractive index of seawater affect the transmission of the\noptical windows, and the effects are different for the windows of the absorption and beam transmission\nsides of the instrument. This effect may also vary slightly between instruments (Van Zee et al. 2002).\n\n4.\n\nTherefore, separate coefficients\n\n\xe2\x88\x82a ( \xce\xbb )\n\nand\n\n\xe2\x88\x82c ( \xce\xbb )\n\ncombine salinity dependent instrument\n\xe2\x88\x82S\n\xe2\x88\x82S\ncharacteristics and water-absorption variations (Pegau et al. 1997; Van Zee et al. 2002), and must be\napplied separately to correct to am ( \xce\xbb ) and cm ( \xce\xbb )\n\nThe temperature and salinity corrections are applied to measured absorption as\nTS\nam ( \xce\xbb ) = am ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92\n\n\xe2\x88\x82aw ( \xce\xbb )\n\xe2\x88\x82T\n\n(T \xe2\x88\x92 Tr ) \xe2\x88\x92\n\n\xe2\x88\x82a ( \xce\xbb )\n\xe2\x88\x82S\n\nS,\n\n(3.12)\n\nand to measured beam attenuation as\nTS\ncm ( \xce\xbb ) = cm ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92\n\n\xe2\x88\x82aw ( \xce\xbb )\n\n(T \xe2\x88\x92 Tr ) \xe2\x88\x92\n\n\xe2\x88\x82c ( \xce\xbb )\n\n(3.13)\nS.\n\xe2\x88\x82T\n\xe2\x88\x82S\nThe temperature dependence coefficients are listed in Table 1.1 (Pegau and Zaneveld 1993; Pegau et al 1997), and\nthe salinity dependence coefficients are provided by the manufacturer (Van Zee et al. 2002). Temperature and\nsalinity must be measured, using a CTD, concurrently with an ac-9 profile to apply these corrections using (3.12)\nand (3.13). Therefore, it is strongly recommended that the ac-9 be mounted on the same profiling package as an\naccurate CTD, and from this perspective, the priority of CTD measurements is higher than is implied in Table 3.1\n(Vol. I, Chapter 3).\nThese corrections become directly significant only at red and near-infrared wavelengths (see Table 1.1).\nTS\nTS\nHowever, the methods of the next section depend on accurate values of am ( \xce\xbb NIR ) and cm ( \xce\xbb NIR ) at a near-infrared\nreference wavelength \xce\xbb NIR \xe2\x89\x88 715 nm to determine scattering corrections at all wavelengths. Moreover, the\ntemperature correction in (3.12) must also be applied to laboratory spectrophotometric measurements of absorption\nby CDOM (Chapter 4), if the temperatures of the pure-water reference blank and filtered seawater sample differ\nsignificantly.\nScattering Corrections\nThe systematic scattering offsets between true absorption and absorption measured with a reflective tube\ninstrument, as described in equations (3.2) and (3.3) and related text in Sect. 3.1, were evaluated by Zaneveld et al.\n(1994). They recommended a hierarchy of three alternative methods for correcting the scattering offsets to the\nTS\ntemperature and salinity corrected measured absorption am ( \xce\xbb ) :\n1.\n\nSubtract the measured absorption at a near infrared reference wavelength, e.g. \xce\xbb NIR \xe2\x89\x88 715 nm for an ac-9,\nor \xce\xbb NIR \xe2\x89\x88 750 nm for measurements with a laboratory spectrophotometer (Chapter 4). After first applying\nthe temperature and salinity corrections using (3.12) and (3.13), assume that the a ( \xce\xbb NIR ) \xe2\x88\x92 aw ( \xce\xbb NIR ) \xe2\x89\x88 0\n\nand that the entire measured signal at the reference wavelength is due to wavelength independent\nscattering errors, so that\nTS\nTS\na ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 aw ( \xce\xbb ) = am ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 am ( \xce\xbb NIR ) .\n\n36\n\n(3.14)\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nTS\nThe value of am ( \xce\xbb NIR ) should be reported with the corrected absorption values when this method is used.\n\n2.\n\nAssuming a wavelength-independent scattering phase function appropriate for the type of particulates in a\ngiven water mass, and a weighting function W ( \xcf\x88 ) based on instrument characteristics [see equation (3.3)\nand the preceding discussion in Sect. 1.1 above], estimate the scattering error as a fraction \xce\xb5 of the\nmeasured scattering coefficient, and subtract it from the measured absorption at each wavelength, i.e.\nTS\nTS\nTS\na ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 aw ( \xce\xbb ) = am ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 \xce\xb5 \xef\xa3\xae cm ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 am ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9 .\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\n(3.15)\n\nBased on analyses of field measurements, laboratory experiments using an ac-9, and theoretical\ncalculations (Kirk 1992), the fraction \xce\xb5 varies from approximately 0.14 for predominately biological\nparticles in the open ocean (Case 1 waters) and increases to approximately 0.18 in waters were scattering\nis dominated by suspended sediments (Case 2 waters). Note that although the scattering correction using\n(3.15) is not sensitive to temperature and salinity corrections at wavelengths < 650 nm, it is nevertheless\nstrongly recommended that the temperature and salinity corrected values be used here as well - if for no\nother reason than facilitating quality control comparisons between corrections made by different methods.\n3.\n\nCombine methods 1 and 2, and the assumptions underlying both methods, to use the ac-9 measurements at\nthe reference wavelength to determine \xce\xb5 , so that (3.15) becomes\nTS\n\xef\xa3\xae\n\xef\xa3\xb9 TS\nam ( \xce\xbb NIR )\nTS\nTS\n(3.16)\na ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 aw ( \xce\xbb ) = am ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 \xef\xa3\xaf TS\n\xef\xa3\xba \xef\xa3\xaecm ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 am ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9 .\nTS\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\xef\xa3\xaf cm ( \xce\xbb NIR ) \xe2\x88\x92 am ( \xce\xbb NIR ) \xef\xa3\xba\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nThe ac-9 instrument design constrains the scattering error fraction to \xce\xb5 \xe2\x89\xa5 0.07 (Kirk 1992), and although\nit is theoretically possible for \xce\xb5 to reach values exceeding 0.5, the authors have not encountered values\ngreater than 0.35.\n\nThe choice of which method should be used to correct scattering errors in ac-9 absorption measurements\ndepends largely on the combination of measured variables, the uncertainty of each measurements, and a judgment of\nhow likely the assumption that a ( \xce\xbb NIR ) \xe2\x88\x92 aw ( \xce\xbb NIR ) \xe2\x89\x88 0 is true in a given water mass.\n1.\n\n2.\n\nIt is strongly recommended to use Method 3 to apply the scattering correction, if all variables in (3.16) have\nbeen measured with acceptable uncertainty, and there is no reason to suspect that particle absorption is\nsignificant at the near-infrared reference wavelength \xce\xbb NIR .\nTS\nTS\nMethod 2 is recommended if both am ( \xce\xbb ) and cm ( \xce\xbb ) are available at one or more visible wavelengths,\nTS\nTS\nbut am ( \xce\xbb NIR ) and cm ( \xce\xbb NIR ) either were not measured with acceptable uncertainties, or there is reason to\n\nsuspect that a ( \xce\xbb NIR ) \xe2\x88\x92 aw ( \xce\xbb NIR ) > 0 .\n3.\n\nTS\nTS\nMethod 1 must be used if only am ( \xce\xbb ) and am ( \xce\xbb NIR ) are measured, as for example, when a laboratory\n\nbenchtop spectrophotometer is used to measure ag ( \xce\xbb ) in filtered water samples (Chapter 4). This situation\nmight also occur if the c side data of an ac-9 were lost due to an electronic failure, or blockage of its flowtube, and one wished to salvage the absorption profile measured with that instrument.\n\n3.7 QUALITY CONTROL PROCEDURES\nThere are several quality assurance tests that can be made to check how well an ac-9 is operating.\nWhen using intake filters for quality control measurements in tests of instrument performance, it is best to filter\nboth sides of an ac-9. This can be done by filtering one side at a time, and doing multiple profiles, or by connecting\na single filter and pump to both sides of the ac-9. When connecting to both sides using a single filter you are\nreducing an already low flow, which makes it more difficult to remove bubbles from the system, but it requires\nfewer casts to complete an instrument test. A degassing Y may be used on the outflow side of the combined\nplumbing arrangement to facilitate bubble removal.\n\n37\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nThe noise level in the measurements should be >0.005 m-1 at all channels. Measurements with noise exceeding\nthis criterion indicate instruments with large measurement noise, a rough check of quality of the calibration, and\npressure or temperature dependencies that may exist.\nAssuming that there is no measurable scattering by particles that pass through the filter then the filtered a and c\nmeasurements should be equal, and departures >0.005 m-1 are symptoms of drift in pure water calibration offsets, or\ncalibration errors due to optical impurity of the reference water.\nComparisons of profiles between successive down and up casts may help in separating suspected temperature\neffects from pressure dependencies. Evidence of hysteresis in different up and down profile responses to gradient\nfeatures is symptomatic of a problem with an instrument\xe2\x80\x99s internal temperature compensation Care must be taken in\nthis interpretation, however, because the upcast water intake is taken in the wake of the rising package and\nturbulence may also cause apparent hysteresis symptoms to appear in the measurements. Pressure effects should not\nhave hysteresis.\nIf more than one ac-9 is available, it is useful to plumb both instruments in identical configurations (both with,\nor without an intake filter) on the same instrument and measure a quick sequence of comparative profiles to\nintercalibrate the instruments. The profile data from the two instruments should be compared only after each has\nbeen fully processed and corrected for temperature, salinity and scattering errors (Sect. 3.4). Discrepancies in this\ntype of comparison cannot indicate which instrument is incorrect, but does provide a clear indication that the\ncalibration of one, or both, is not correct.\n\nREFERENCES\nJames, H.R., and E.A. Greg Mitchell, Mati Kahru, John Wieland and Malgorzata Stramska\nScripps Institution of Oceanography, University of California San Diego, California\n\n4.1 INTRODUCTION\nThe spectral absorption coefficient is one of the inherent optical properties that influence the reflectance of\n\naquatic systems. The absorption coefficient a ( \xce\xbb ) , in m , at any point within a natural water body can be described\n-1\n\nin terms of the additive contribution of its components as\n\na ( \xce\xbb ) = aw ( \xce\xbb ) + ap ( \xce\xbb ) + ag ( \xce\xbb ) , m-1,\n\n(4.1)\n\nwhere aw ( \xce\xbb ) , ap ( \xce\xbb ) and ag ( \xce\xbb ) are the spectral absorption coefficients of water, particles, and soluble\ncomponents, respectively. The spectral absorption coefficients of pure water adopted for the protocols are given in\nTable 1.1 (Ch. 1), and combine the results of Pope and Fry (1997), Sogandares and Fry (1997), Fry (2000) and Kou\net al. (1993). The depth (z) dependence of the absorption coefficients is omitted for brevity. The particle absorption\ncoefficient may be further decomposed as\n\nap ( \xce\xbb ) = a\xcf\x86 ( \xce\xbb ) + ad ( \xce\xbb ) , m-1,\n\n(4.2)\n\nwhere a\xcf\x86 ( \xce\xbb ) and ad ( \xce\xbb ) are the spectral absorption coefficients of phytoplankton, and de-pigmented particles,\nrespectively. Laboratory methods are described for determining operational estimates of these fractions. It is\nconceptually possible to further separate ad ( \xce\xbb ) into absorption fractions due to de-pigmented organic and\n\ninorganic particles, but at present, there are no well established protocols for separately determining the absorption\ncoefficient for inorganic particles.\nTo interpret aquatic spectral reflectance and better understand photochemical and photobiological processes in\nnatural waters, it is essential to quantify the contributions of the individual constituents to the total absorption\ncoefficients in the ultraviolet (UV) and visible region of the spectrum. The protocols presented here are based on\nthe evolution, starting with articles by Kalle (1938) and Yentsch (1962), of methods for analyzing the absorption by\nsoluble and particulate material in natural waters. Laboratory measurements and data analysis protocols are\ndescribed for separating the total spectral absorption coefficient, a ( \xce\xbb ) , into its components by spectrophotometric\nmeasurements of samples prepared from filtration of discrete water samples.\nThe spectral absorbance of the filters and filtrate from these samples, as measured in a spectrophotometer, are\nexpressed in units of Optical Density ( OD ), defined as OD ( \xce\xbb ) = Log 10 \xef\xa3\xaeVo ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9 \xe2\x88\x92 Log 10 \xef\xa3\xaeVt ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9 . Vo ( \xce\xbb ) is the\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\nspectrometer response for spectral flux transmitted through the reference material and Vt ( \xce\xbb ) is the response for\n\nspectral flux transmitted through the sample. For the methods presented here the reference is either a properly\nhydrated GF/F blank filter for particle absorption, or a clean quartz glass optical cuvette filled directly from a\n\n39\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\npurified water source for soluble material absorption. Note that OD is a dimensionless quantity. The use of base10 logarithms in this context is a carryover from common practice in chemical spectroscopy and is the typical output\nof commercial spectrophotometers routinely used for these methods. Therefore, it is necessary to convert the OD\nmeasurements described in this chapter to the base-e representation of absorbance, i.e. to multiply OD by 2.303, to\nconform to the convention used throughout the ocean optics protocols. In general, these protocols are written\nassuming that the instrument that is used directly computes the optical density of the sample relative to the\nappropriate reference sample.\nThere has been considerable research to develop robust protocols that provide the most accurate estimates of\nabsorption for various material fractions in natural waters. NASA-sponsored workshops were held at Scripps\nInstitution of Oceanography and Bigelow Laboratory for Ocean Sciences to review absorption protocols, evaluate\ninstrumentation, and define areas of consensus as well as areas of uncertainty that warrant further research (Mitchell\net al. 2000).\nThe most widely used approach for estimating absorption by particulate matter in water samples involves\nanalysis of the particles concentrated on filters (Yentsch, 1957). Absorption of phytoplankton suspensions\ndetermined using procedures that capture most of the forward scattered light (Shibata, 1958) can be related to the\nabsorption measured on the filters to make quantitative corrections for the pathlength amplification effect (\xce\xb2) caused\nby the highly scattering filter medium (Duntley, 1942; Butler, 1962). The pathlength amplification parameter was\nsymbolized as \xce\xb2 by Kiefer and SooHoo (1982) following the nomenclature of Butler (1962). This symbol should\nnot to be confused with the volume scattering coefficient \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb , \xce\xa8 ) used in other chapters of this Technical\nMemorandum.\nKiefer and SooHoo (1982) reported a constant to scale the red peak of chlorophyll absorption for natural\nparticles retained on GF/C filters to the diffuse absorption coefficients determined on suspensions by Kiefer et al.\n(1979). The diffuse absorption coefficient is double the value of the volume absorption coefficient of interest here\n(Preisendorfer, 1976). Mitchell and Kiefer (1984, 1988a) made direct estimates of volume absorption coefficients\nfor phytoplankton suspensions and absorbance on glass fiber filters with the same particles to develop empirical\nequations that relate the amplification factor to the glass fiber sample optical density. This procedure is the basis of\nmost laboratory methods for determining particle absorption in water samples.\nField applications of these quantitative estimates of ap ( \xce\xbb ) were reported by Mitchell and Kiefer (1984,\n1988b) and Bricaud and Stramski (1990). More detailed empirical results to correct for pathlength amplification\nwere reported by Mitchell (1990) for various filter types and diverse cultures coccoid cyanobacteria,\nnanochlorophytes, diatoms, chrysophytes and dinoflagellates with sizes ranging from 2 \xc2\xb5m to 20 \xc2\xb5m. Cleveland\nand Weidemann (1993) and Tassan and Ferrari (1995) found that the empirical relationships of Mitchell (1990) were\nconsistent with similar types of phytoplankton, but Moore et al. (1995) reported large differences in the\namplification factor for Synechococcus sp. (WH8103) and Prochlorococcus marinus that were about half the size of\nthe smallest cells studied by Mitchell (1990). Similar results were obtained by Allali et al. (1997) for natural\npopulations of the Equatorial Pacific dominated by picoplankton. For samples with substantial turbidity and\nscattering due to inorganic matter (coastal, shelf, coccolithophore blooms), methods to correct for resulting artifacts\nhave been described by Tassan and Ferrari (1995a, 1995b). Table 4.1 provides a summary of various published\nresults for pathlength amplification factors.\nSeparation of the particle fraction into phytoplankton and other components is of considerable ecological and\nbiogeochemical interest. Early efforts to separate absorbing components for natural particles included treatment\nwith organic solvents, UV radiation, and potassium permanganate (references can be found in Shifrin, 1988, and\nBricaud and Stramski, 1990). The most widely used chemical method is based on methanol extraction (Kishino et\nal. 1985, 1986). A recent method consists of bleaching the phytoplankton pigments by sodium hypochlorite (Tassan\nand Ferrari, 1995a; Ferrari and Tassan, 1999). Spectral fluorescence methods to estimate the fraction of\nphotosynthetically active absorption, if separate total particulate absorption has been determined, have been reported\nby Sosik and Mitchell, (1995).\nSoluble absorption observations were described by Bricaud et al. (1981) for diverse ocean environments,\nincluding oligotrophic and eutrophic regions. Other field reports can be found in the references listed in more recent\narticles (Carder et al., 1989a, 1989b; Blough et al., 1993; Vodacek et al., 1996; Hoge et al., 1993; Nelson et al.,\n1998; D\xe2\x80\x99Sa et al., 1999). Spectrophotometric measurement of absorption by dissolved materials is straightforward,\n\n40\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nbut has limits due to the very small signal for short pathlengths routinely employed (usually 10 cm), and to\ndifficulties in maintaining quality control of purified water used as a reference.\nThis chapter defines protocols for the operational determinations of absorption coefficients for particulate and\nsoluble matter in water samples. Methods are specified for separating particulate and soluble material by filtration,\npartitioning total particulate absorption into contributions by phytoplankton and de-pigmented particles (detritus),\nand corrections for pathlength amplification due to semi-diffuse transmittance of the filters. Recommendations are\nmade based on widely accepted methods and processing procedures. NASA-sponsored workshops have confirmed\nvarious aspects of previously reported methods (Mitchell et al. 2000).\n\n4.2 SAMPLE ACQUISITION\nWater samples should be taken using Niskin (or similar) bottles at the site of, and simultaneously with, the\nsurface in-water optical measurements, and at depth increments sufficient to resolve variability within at least the\ntop optical depth. When possible, samples should be acquired at several depths distributed throughout the upper\n300m of the water column (or in turbid water, up to seven diffuse attenuation depths for PAR irradiance,\nln(E(0)/E(z))=7), to provide a basis for relating the spectroscopic measurements of absorption to in situ profile\nmeasurements. Samples should be drawn immediately from the in situ sampling bottles into clean sampling bottles\nusing clean silicon rubber or Tygon tubing or by directly filling the sample bottles from the Niskin bottle spigot. If\nNiskin bottles will not be sampled immediately, precautions must be taken to ensure large particles that settle are resuspended. This can be done by transferring all water from the Niskin to a bottle or carboy larger than the total\nvolume of the Niskin so that the entire water sample can be mixed (invert bottle numerous times to mix by\nturbulence), or by draining a small amount of water from the Niskin and manually inverting the entire Niskin prior\nto sub-sampling. Sample bottles should be kept cool (ideally near in situ temperatures), and dark prior to sample\npreparations. Preparations should be completed as soon as possible after sampling, but no later than several hours\nafter the sample was acquired.\n\n4.3 SPECTROPHOTOMETER CHARACTERISTICS AND CALIBRATION\nA spectrophotometer used for absorption measurements following the protocols presented in this chapter must\nmeet the following minimum performance specifications:\n1.\n\nThe unit\xe2\x80\x99s monochromator, or spectrograph, must yield a Full-Width at Half-Maximum (FWHM)\nbandwidth \xe2\x89\xa4 4 nm . A larger FWHM bandwidth will not adequately resolve the red chlorophyll a\nabsorption band.\n\n2.\n\nThe instrument\xe2\x80\x99s baseline spectrum characteristics, specified below, must be maintained over the range\nfrom 300 nm to 850 nm for measuring absorption by particles concentrated on filters, and from 250 nm to\n850 nm for measuring absorption by dissolved materials.\n\n3.\n\nFor measuring absorption by particles concentrated on filters, baseline noise must be <0.01 OD , and noise\n<0.005 OD is strongly recommended.\n\n4.\n\nFor measuring absorption by dissolved materials, baseline noise must be <0.001 OD units, and noise\n<0.0005 OD is strongly recommended.\n\n5.\n\nThe instrument\xe2\x80\x99s baseline spectrum must be relatively flat over the wavelength range of interest and its\nshape and magnitude must be stable over time. Any tendencies for the spectral shape and magnitude of an\ninstrument\xe2\x80\x99s baseline to drift must be well-behaved and slow enough that the rate of baseline drift may be\ncharacterized with an uncertainty less than the noise levels specified above. It is recommended to check\nthe instrument baseline at intervals of 1 hr to 2 hr during an extended series of measurements.\n\nOther desirable, but not absolutely essential, spectrophotometer features are variable slit width (to allow reducing\nthe FWHM spectral resolution, when desired), automatic baseline corrections (the adequacy of which must\nnevertheless be verified), and automatic spectral calibration during instrument warm-up (using mercury emission\nlines supplied by an internal lamp source).\nThe spectral accuracy of the spectrometer should be verified by scanning a holmium oxide filter, with reference\nto an air-to-air baseline. This spectral calibration should be repeated each time the instrument is turned on, and at\n\n41\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nthe conclusion of a series of measurements. Alternatively, a spectrophotometer\xe2\x80\x99s spectral characteristics may be\ncalibrated using an internal line source (e.g. a mercury lamp), if the instrument is so equipped, but independent\nchecks with the holmium oxide filter are also strongly advised. A set of absorbance reference filter standards, of\nknown OD , must be used to calibrate a spectrophotometer\xe2\x80\x99s responses over the range of OD associated with the\nsamples to be measured. This calibration, together with instrument baseline spectrum determinations, should be\nrepeated at intervals necessary to characterize (within the noise tolerances given above) any measurable drifts in the\ninstrument baseline and/or OD response. Unless the investigator can confirm the stability of the instrument that is\nused, these calibration procedures should be repeated each time the spectrophotometer is turned on. As a minimum\nthey should be performed at any time there is a change of lamp source, blocking filter, or other instrument setup\ncharacteristic that affects the optical response and on a regular basis during routine work.\nThe present version of these protocols is written assuming the use of a commercial dual-beam\nspectrophotometer, with the sample and reference targets illuminated by the collimated output of a grating\nmonochromator. The protocols also apply, with minimal modifications, to measurements using a single-beam\nmonochromator or otherwise similar optical configuration. Mitchell et al. (2000) report comparisons between OD\nmeasurements of a common set of GF/F filtered particle samples using several spectrophotometers of these types, as\nwell as spectrophotometers based on very different optical configurations. For a diatom culture measured during the\nScripps workshop, several commercial dual beam spectrophotometers estimated sample filter OD spectra\nconsistently within 5 % (Figure 4.1). Some of the differently configured instruments were within 10 % of the\nselected reference dual-beam instrument but in some cases had limited spectral range either in the UV or the nearinfrared, or both (data not shown). The largest divergence was found for a grating spectrograph instrument that\nilluminates the filter with diffuse white light (Figure 4.1). This unit yielded OD values that were significantly\nhigher than, albeit linearly related to (with a slope of approximately 0.7), the OD measurements made with\nconventional dual beam spectrophotometers (Mitchell et al. 2000). This result indicates that the pathlength\namplification (\xce\xb2 factor) associated with this instrument is significantly different from previously published values\n(Table 4.1), which were derived using conventional dual beam spectrophotometers. An investigator using this type\nof spectrophotometer, or another design with yet a different optical configuration, must either compare filter OD\nspectra measurements to reference measurements on the same filters with a \xe2\x80\x9cconventional\xe2\x80\x9d spectrophotometer to\nderive an OD scaling function, or otherwise determine pathlength amplification factors for the instrument\nconfiguration, using methods discussed in Mitchell et al. (2000) and references cited therein.\n\n4.4 PARTICLE ABSORPTION: SAMPLE FILTER PREPARATION AND\nANALYSIS\nThe procedures described in this section are recommended for determining the spectral absorption coefficients\nof particles in discrete samples of natural waters. These laboratory measurements are complementary to methods for\nmeasuring in situ profiles of absorption, as described in Chapter 3 of this volume, and provide additional\ninformation on the partition of particle absorption by phytoplankton and other particles. Water samples are filtered\nand absorbance spectra of the filter, OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) ,are estimated for the retained particles using a laboratory\nspectrophotometer. After measurement, the sample filters are soaked in chemical solvents to extract, or bleach,\nphytoplankton pigments (Kishino et al., 1985; Tassan and Ferrari 1995a) then rinsed to remove the chemicals and\npigments from the material retained on the filter. The OD fd ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum of the filter is then determined in the\nspectrophotometer to obtain the absorption component of the de-pigmented particles, which are sometimes referred\nto as detritus or tripton. Depending on the method used to de-pigment the samples, this fraction also includes\nbleached cells and phycobilipigments that are not extractable in methanol and also inorganic minerals that may be\nimportant absorbers in some water samples. The raw OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) and OD fd ( \xce\xbb ) data are used to calculate total\nparticulate and de-pigmented absorption coefficients ap ( \xce\xbb ) and ad ( \xce\xbb ) , respectively. The absorption coefficient of\nphytoplankton, a\xcf\x86 ( \xce\xbb ) , is then calculated as the difference ap ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 ad ( \xce\xbb ) .\nFiltration\nThe Whatman GF/F\xef\x9b\x9b filter (which is binder-free and combustible, with a nominal pore size of 0.7 \xc2\xb5m) is\nrecommended for particle absorption sampling. This type of filter is also recommended by (JGOFS 1991) for\n\n42\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nvarious particulate and pigment analyses. Some authors have reported that particulate material less than 0.7 \xc2\xb5m in\nsize will not be retained by the GF/F filter, and that this fraction may contain up to 10 % or 15 % of the\nphytoplankton biomass as measured by chlorophyll concentration. Chavez et al. (1995), however, found no\nstatistical difference between GF/F and 0.2 \xc2\xb5m filters for chlorophyll and productivity measurements. The\nabsorption of particles having diameters between 0.22 \xc2\xb5m and 0.7 \xc2\xb5m may be selectively determined by filtering the\nGF/F filtrate again through a 0.22 \xc2\xb5m Millipore cellulose acetate membrane filter, and measuring its absorbance\nwith a spectrophotometer (Ferrari and Tassan, 1996). Note that Mitchell (1990) reported pathlength amplification\nfactors for cellulose acetate filters that are substantially different than those for GF/F filters and also described the\nrelative difficulty of keeping cellulose acetate filters properly hydrated.\nThe optical transparency of the GF/F filter relative to air decreases significantly below 380 nm but many\nspectrophotometers can still make optical density determinations to 300 nm with these filters. The transparency of\nthe filter also increases with hydration; so all samples must be fully - but not excessively - hydrated for proper\nperformance of analytical procedures and accurate optical corrections. Pre-soaking GF/F filters 1 to 2 hrs before use\ncan lead to less variability between individual filters (Bricaud and Stramski 1990). For oceanic water samples,\nseawater filtered through a 0.2 \xc2\xb5m filter should be used to hydrate the filters. Freshly filtered seawater should be\nused since water that is left standing in clear containers may grow considerable amounts of algae over relatively\nshort periods of time if there are any nutrients in the filtered seawater. For fresh inland water samples, purified fresh\nwater may be used.\nGlass fiber, cellulose acetate, and other strongly diffusing filters have large scattering coefficients, which\nincrease the optical path length of photons in the measurement beam. Filtration volume Vf should be adjusted so\nthat the optical densities of the filter samples, relative to the blank filter satisfy the criteria that\n0.05 < OD fp ( 675 ) \xe2\x89\xa4 0.25 and OD fp ( 440 ) \xe2\x89\xa4 0.4 OD (Mitchell 1990). Optical density spectra of the sample filters\nshould be measured as soon as possible following filtration, because pigment decomposition may occur (Stramski\n1990). If filters must be stored, immediately place the unfolded filters into flat tissue containers designed for liquid\nnitrogen storage. Liquid nitrogen storage is recommended because alternative freezing methods were shown to have\nmore artifacts in comparison tests (Sosik, 1999).\na. Sample Filter Preparation\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCollect water samples, and maintain them in the dark at, or near, in situ water temperature.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPrepare 0.2 \xc2\xb5m filtered seawater (FSW) in sufficient volume for hydrating sample and blank filters.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSet up and maintain the filter apparatus in dim light to minimize photodegradation of the samples.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFor each sample, place a GF/F filter onto the filtration rig. Also prepare two blank GF/F filters by soaking\nthem in ~25 ml of 0.2 \xc2\xb5m filtered water while mounted on the filtration funnel (with valves closed) during\nthe sample filtration.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFilter the samples on GF/F filters under low vacuum (~125 mm Hg).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFilter a sufficient volume of water Vf to yield sample optical density relative to the blank filter in the range\nspecified above. For field samples collected in the upper 100-150 m and filtered onto 25 mm GF/F filters,\nVf is typically in the range 0.5 L to 5 L, depending on the in situ density concentration of particles.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDo not let the preparations run dry during filtration. Turn off the vacuum to each sample as it completes\nfiltering. Immediately place samples on a drop of 0.2 \xc2\xb5m FSW in the appropriate container, depending on\nhow they will be stored.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRecord the filter and filtration funnel type, the diameter Df of the area on the filter that contains the\nconcentrated particles, and the volume of water filtered Vf.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMeasure the absorption spectra in a spectrophotometer, or store the filters in liquid nitrogen, as soon as\npossible.\n\n43\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nb. Sample Filter Storage\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIf the filter samples will be analyzed immediately, store each filter in a labeled petri dish (e.g. Gelman\xef\x9b\x9b\nsnap-top dishes). Ensure proper hydration of the sample by placing the GF/F filter on a small drop of 0.2\n\xc2\xb5m FSW. Store each filter sample in the dark and refrigerate it (~4 deg. C) until it is to be measured in the\nspectrophotometer.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIf the spectrophotometric measurements will be delayed more than 24 hours following sample filtration, the\nfilter samples should be prepared for liquid nitrogen storage. Samples should be stored in containers that\nallow the filter to remain flat, and which are specifically designed for immersion in liquid nitrogen (e.g.\nFisher Histoprep\xef\x9b\x9b tissue capsules). One pair of blank filters should also be prepared each day for use as\nthe reference blank for samples collected that day. Samples may be stored in liquid nitrogen for extended\nperiods, but it is strongly recommended to analyze them as soon as possible.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nNon-pressurized liquid nitrogen dewars generally retain liquid nitrogen for 2-4 weeks. Pressurized liquid\nnitrogen dewars can be rented at low cost for extended cruises (4-5 weeks) so that the sample dewars may\nbe replenished and kept full. Care must be taken at sea, and in return shipping, to ensure that the samples\nare properly frozen. Samples should be shipped in liquid nitrogen dry shippers, which will maintain proper\ntemperatures for 2 to 3 weeks, if they are properly charged and in good condition.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAir transport of liquid nitrogen dry shippers is approved under International Air Transportation Agreement\n(IATA 41st Edition Section A800; US Federal Aviation Administration Dangerous Good Bulletin DGAB98-03; August 25, 1998). That approval notwithstanding, many investigators have experienced difficulties\nin clearing customs, and in transport of liquid nitrogen dry shippers via commercial airfreight, or as\nchecked baggage. The investigator should contact the carriers in advance and provide the IATA approval\nand FAA bulletins pertaining to liquid nitrogen dry shipper transport. If the dry shipper is to be transported\nas checked baggage, advanced coordination with the airline is strongly recommended to avoid confiscation\nof samples and delays in return shipment. When samples are shipped as checked baggage or freight, the\nIATA memo, DOT memo, and manufacturer\xe2\x80\x99s certificate should be affixed to the dry shipper to minimize\npotential delays.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nTemporary storage of filter samples on dry ice can be considered during transport. But maximum duration\nof dry ice in insulated shipping boxes is several days, so the use of liquid nitrogen dry shippers is strongly\nrecommended.\n\nDetermination of spectral optical density of sample filters\nAfter preparation, the optical density spectrum of each sample filter is measured using a laboratory\nspectrophotometer. The performance characteristics and calibration requirements of the spectrophotometer used for\nthese measurements are described above in Section 4.3.\na. Reference Blank Spectra\nWith a dual beam spectrophotometer, two reference filter blanks saturated with FSW are used to measure the\nreference spectrum, and one is left in the reference beam during sample measurements. For typical single beam\ninstruments, generally the reference is scanned, and then samples are placed into the beam and scanned. Most\nmodern spectrophotometers, whether single or double beam, automatically store the instrument\xe2\x80\x99s reference spectrum\nand recorded sample spectra are automatically corrected to yield OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) relative to the reference blank filter. A\nnew instrument reference baseline scan should be measured each time the spectrophotometer is powered up, and\nwhenever its configuration has been changed. The baseline should also be checked regularly (every 1 hr to 2 hr)\nduring extended periods of analysis. Frequency of baseline verification will depend on the performance and stability\nof each instrument and should be determined by the investigator prior to executing routine work. Uncorrected\nbaseline drift, and changes in sorting filters or lamp source can cause systematic measurement anomalies.\nWavelength accuracy and measurement precision should also be checked during the analyses (Sect. 4.3 above).\n\n44\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nb. Spectrophotometric Measurement Procedure\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nWarm up the spectrophotometer for 30 minutes.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMeasure the initial instrument baseline and wavelength calibration.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIf using frozen samples, remove the filters from the storage container and place them in petri dishes on\nFSW to ensure hydration. Allow the samples to thaw for approximately 5 min and then refrigerate them in\nthe dark until each filter is ready for analysis.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAn instrument-specific sample-mounting device is recommended to hold filters against a quartz glass\nmounting plate. These mounts should be secure when placed in the sample compartment and hold the\nsample perpendicular to the illumination beam so only the filter and the quartz plate are in the beam.\nUsually, these mounts must be custom fabricated specifically for each different instrument.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nClean the quartz faceplates of the mounting device with purified water and detergent if needed. Rinse them\nwith purified water and ethanol, and dry them thoroughly using lint-free laboratory tissues.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSet the appropriate instrument parameters according to the manufacturer\xe2\x80\x99s instructions.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMount two pre-soaked and water saturated blank filters (one for the sample beam, and one for the reference\nbeam).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nTo test for proper filter hydration, confirm that there is a drop of FSW left on the mounting plate when the\nfilter is lifted. With the filter on the mounting plate there should be a slight sheen on the top surface of the\nfilter, and a very narrow (~1 mm) border of water around the edges of the filter. Be careful not to use too\nmuch water, or the sample may wash away.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nExamine the back of the filter on the mounting plate to be sure that no bubbles are trapped between the\nfilter and the quartz glass plate on the sample holder. There should be a uniform layer of water between the\nfilter and quartz glass mounting plate. If bubbles are present, which will be obvious, pick up the filter with\nforceps, and replace it on the plate with a slight dragging motion across a drop of filtered seawater. Reinspect the back of the filter and repeat the foregoing procedure until no bubbles are present. Adjust the\namount of FSW as necessary to ensure proper hydration.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAlternative mounts that expose both sides of the filter to air may be used to avoid bubbles altogether.\nSample hydration is more difficult to maintain when using this type of filter mount so the investigator must\ndevelop a satisfactory procedure to ensure proper hydration of sample and reference.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRun the instrument baseline correction using the two blanks. For most commercial units, this baseline will\nbe automatically used as the reference to calculate OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) . Immediately after the baseline correction is\nfinished, and without touching the blank filters, run the two blanks as a sample scan to confirm that\nbaseline performance is within acceptable tolerance over the spectral range of determination (Sect. 4.3\nabove). This spectrum should be flat spectrally. Baseline noise less than \xc2\xb1 0.005 OD is recommended.\nSave this scan for confirmation of instrument performance. If a spectrally flat baseline cannot be achieved\nover the spectral range of interest, the stored baseline must be subtracted from subsequent measurements of\nsample filter OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) .\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIf using a single beam instrument, or instruments run in the single beam mode the blank is not kept in the\ninstrument so one does not need to rehydrate the blank reference filter regularly. Most modern single beam\nspectrophotometers will also automatically use the blank reference stored in memory for estimates\nof OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) .\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRemove the blank filter from the quartz glass sample mount in the measurement beam, and replace it with a\nsample filter, ensuring proper hydration of the sample (see above).\nMeasure the sample\nOD fp ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum, save it in a digital file, and record all relevant information.\n\n45\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe blank reference filter will dry out over time, and must be hydrated regularly. If absorption signal\ndeviates significantly from zero (more than 0.02 OD ) in the infrared (750-800 nm), this often indicates a\ndry reference or sample filter. If using a quartz plate, check the reference filter after every 5-6 scans, and\nhydrate as needed. If the filters are mounted in air, hydrate the blank before every scan.\n\nSample Filter Preparation for De-pigmented Particle Absorption\nAfter preparing an ap ( \xce\xbb ) filter sample and determining its OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum on the spectrophotometer, the\nsample should be processed to remove its pigments and determine ad ( \xce\xbb ) . The shape of the ad ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum\nusually decreases monotonically with wavelength, following exponential form that is flatter than the shape of the\nsoluble absorption spectrum. Since the goal is generally to get an estimate of phytoplankton absorption, if there is a\nresidual chlorophyll a absorption peak in the red near 675 nm, the extraction process should be repeated to remove\nit. Variations of this method include use of hot or boiling methanol and varying extraction times. Use of hot\nmethanol has risks due to flammability, and volatility. If this process is used, extra precautions must be taken.\nBleaching of the organic pigments can also be accomplished for situations with difficult to extract pigments\nincluding phycobilins or other chemically polar pigments that do not extract well in methanol. Pigment extraction in\na chemical solvent, such as methanol, is a fundamentally different chemical process than bleaching the pigments\nusing sodium hypochlorite (NaClO). Bleaching involves placing a small amount of 0.1 % active chlorine solution\nonto the filter, then rinsing it off with FSW. The NaClO oxidizes the pigment molecules, making their light\nabsorption negligible. FSW rinses then remove the excess NaClO, which absorbs negligibly at wavelengths\n>400 nm, but absorbs strongly at shorter wavelengths. The bleaching method of pigment removal has been shown\nto be effective in situations where methanol cannot be used, as on cellulose membranes such as the 0.22 \xc2\xb5m\nMillipore filter, or when phycobilins are present (Tassan and Ferrari 1995a; Mitchell et al. 2000). This procedure\ncan also be adapted for use with particulate suspensions.\nNeither methanol extraction, nor NaClO oxidation, provides an ideal means of separating particulate absorption\ninto \xe2\x80\x98algal\xe2\x80\x99 and \xe2\x80\x98detrital\xe2\x80\x99 components. In each case, the action of the chemical agent is not well understood, and in\nmany situations the two methods will yield very different results. The decision to apply either the bleaching, or\nmethanol extraction, method will depend on the situation. For example, in inland waters where either cyanobacteria,\nor chlorophytes, are dominant, the bleaching technique is preferred, because of the presence of phycobilins and of\nextraction resistant algae (e.g. Porra 1990). In coastal oceanic waters, on the other hand, the methanol technique is\npreferred, because the results will be comparable to previously published results and there is no particular advantage\nto using bleach. In open-ocean samples (e.g. the Sargasso Sea), however, absorption by phycobilins is small, but\npresent in some particulate absorption samples and in methanol-extracted filters (N.B. Nelson unpublished data).\nThe methanol technique will provide results which are comparable to earlier studies, but with errors due to\nincomplete extraction and wavelength shifts in the phycobilin absorption bands.\na. Methanol Extraction method\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nReplace the sample and blank filters on the filtration system. Treat blank filters exactly as if they were\nsample filters.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAdd 5 mL to 10 mL of 100 % methanol to each filter by gently pouring it down the sides of the filter funnel\nto minimize resuspension of the sample particles, and let stand for 1 min.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFilter the methanol through the sample, turn off the vacuum, close the valves and add 10 - 15 mL of\nmethanol.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAllow the sample to stand in methanol for approximately 1 hr. Do not allow the filter to go dry during the\nextraction period. Time of extraction will vary depending on the filter load and phytoplankton species\ncomposition. Place aluminum foil over the filtration cups to minimize contamination during extraction.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAfter extraction is complete, turn on the vacuum and draw the methanol and dissolved pigments through\nthe filter. Rinse the sides of the filter tower twice with small amounts of methanol. Finally, rinse the sides\nof the filter tower three times with ~20 mL of 0.2 \xc2\xb5m FSW. Also rinse the blanks with FSW after methanol\nextraction to minimize filter dehydration during spectrophotometric analysis.\n\n46\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPigment extraction is complete when the 675 nm chlorophyll a absorption peak is not present in the\nOD fd ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSuccessive, short extractions of 10 minutes can sometimes improve the pigment extraction.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPhycobilins, and some eukaryotic pigments, will not be extracted efficiently by methanol.\n\nb. Sodium Hypochlorite oxidation method\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPrepare NaClO solution:\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFor freshwater samples: 0.1 % active chlorine in purified water (e.g. Milli-Q water).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFor marine samples: 0.1 % active chlorine in purified water containing 60 gl-1 Na2SO4, to match osmotic\npressure of sample cells.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe volume of 0.1% active chlorine solution needed to bleach pigments from a filter sample has been\nempirically shown to be approximately 3OD fp ( 440 ) mL .\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPlace the sample, particle side up, on the filtration system (closed valves).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nGently pour the solution down the sides of the filter funnel.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nLet the solution act for 5 min to 10 min, adding solution as necessary to compensate for loss through the\nfilter.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCover the filtration cup with aluminum foil to prevent contamination during bleaching.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRinse the sample by gentle filtration of 50 mL of water (either fresh water or FSW, depending on sample\nsource).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nComplete bleaching of the pigments is indicated by the absence of a 675 nm peak, together with a concave\nshape near 440 nm, in the OD fd ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum of the bleached filter. If evidence of residual pigment\nabsorption persists, repeat the NaClO oxidation treatment, as indicated above.\n\nSpectrophotometric Measurement of De-pigmented Optical Density Spectra\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe OD fd ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum of the de-pigmented samples should be measured in the spectrophotometer, as\ndescribed above for OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) .\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nNote that methanol-extracted sample and blank filters will tend to dry out quickly if the methanol is not\nthoroughly rinsed from the filters prior to spectrophotometric measurements.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nNaClO oxidized sample and reference filters must be thoroughly rinsed with FSW (or fresh water for\ninland water samples) to extend the spectral range below 400 nm.\n\n4.5 SOLUBLE ABSORPTION SAMPLE PREPARATION AND ANALYSIS\nThe measurement methods described in this section are used to determine ag ( \xce\xbb ) , the spectral absorption\ncoefficient spectrum of gelbstoff, often referred to as dissolved organic matter (CDOM). Water samples are\ncollected and particulate material is removed by filtration. The absorption of the filtrate is measured, relative to\npurified water, using a spectrophotometer. All equipment utilized to prepare soluble absorption samples must\nminimize contamination by organic, or otherwise colored, material. Samples must be protected from photodegradation during preparation and measurements. Plastic or glass filtration apparatus may be used, provided that\nthe units are equipped with mesh filter supports made either of stainless steel or plastic, and not with ground glass\nfrits. Glass frits tend to become clogged over time, and may cause uneven distribution on the filter, reduce the rate\nof filtration and may contaminate the sample filtrate.\n\n47\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nMembrane filters with 0.2 \xc2\xb5m pore size (e.g., Nuclepore\xef\x9b\x9b polycarbonate filters) are recommended for this\nprocedure. The membrane filters should be pre-soaked in 10% HCl, rinsed with 75-100 mL of freshly purified\nwater, and rinsed again with a 75 \xe2\x80\x93 100 mL of the sample before it is used. Tests with purified water have shown\nthat all filters leach contamination that resembles soluble absorption (data not shown). Using polycarbonate\nmembrane filters, an acid soak, pure water rinse and sample rinse minimizes this contamination. Still, we have\nfound the sample preparation procedure increases the apparent absorption spectra of purified water that is prepared\nas though it were a sample when referenced to purified water drawn directly into the measuring cuvette from the\npure water system. Therefore correction for this sample preparation blank is recommended.\nGlass fiber filters should be avoided if possible because they have been shown to cause rather severe\ncontamination of the filtrate in tests using purified water. For samples collected from very turbid waters, glass fiber\nfilters have routinely been used as a pre-filter to minimize clogging of the final filtration with a membrane filter\n(Kowalczuk, 1999). In such cases the investigator must develop a procedure to rinse the glass fiber filter to ensure\nthat the contamination from this method is minimized. Since situations requiring pre-filtration often coincide with\nlarge soluble absorption coefficients, the effects may be easily corrected but it is the responsibility of the\ninvestigator to demonstrate this. Careful assessment of the contamination of any method, and proper corrections\nmust be carried out and reported.\nPreviously we recommended the use of amber-colored borosilicate glass bottles (e.g. Qorpak\xef\x9b\x9b bottles), that\nscreen ambient light, for sample preparation and to store laboratory prepared standard water. However, recent work\n(details not shown) indicate that the amber bottles may leach some colored material into the purified standard water\nthat is prepared before cruises and used to assess the quality of purified water prepared at sea. Therefore we now\nrecommend use of clear borosilicate Qorpak\xef\x9b\x9b bottles (or equivalent) for sample preparations and for the\npreparation of the standard reference water. Prior to each experiment, all filtration apparatus and storage bottles\nshould be thoroughly cleaned.\nPurified water for soluble absorption measurements\nPurified water freshly drawn from a water purification system, such as the Millipore Milli-Q, Millipore AlphaQ, and Barnstead Nanopure units, or their equivalent, is strongly recommended for use at sea in preparing pure\nwater for absorption reference, blanks and for equipment rinses specified in these protocols. Mitchell et al. (2000)\ncompared the water-to-air baseline reference of purified water prepared with these three water purification systems.\nAll three systems provided similar results in baseline tests relative to air at wavelengths between 300 nm and\n900 nm, while small differences were found below 300 nm. It is also recommended to prepare a set of standard\npurified water samples prior to a field deployment as a reference to check daily for pure-water system degradation,\ne.g. due to poor quality feed water.
