Even though bottled purified water standards have been found to deteriorate\nslightly over time, especially from 250 nm to 325 nm, they provide invaluable quality control and an alternative\nsource of reference water in situations when the purification system performance degrades dramatically.\nPre-cruise preparations\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSample bottles (clear borosilicate Qorpak\xef\x9b\x9b with polyethylene lined caps) used to collect sample filtrate or\nto store standard reference water need to be thoroughly cleaned in advance to remove any potential organic\ncontaminants. Sequential soaks and rinses in dilute detergent, purified water, and 10 % HCl, followed by a\nfinal copious rinse in purified water, are recommended.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRinse plastic caps with 10 % HCl, twice with freshly prepared purified water (e.g. using a Millipore AlphaQ system), and dry them at 70o C for 4 hr to 6 hr.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCombust bottles with aluminum foil covers at 450o C for 4 hr to 6 hr.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFill clean, combusted bottles with fresh purified water drawn directly from the purification unit.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAssemble the combusted bottles and clean caps. Store in the dark.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThese standards are used daily during cruises to evaluate the quality of purified water freshly prepared at\nsea.\n\n48\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThis carefully prepared standard water sometimes must be used as the reference material for actual sample\nanalysis. If this is planned, the investigator should determine the optical density of the standard water\npreparations before and after a cruise relative to fresh purified water drawn directly into the quartz cuvettes.\nAn assessment of the change in this water over time may indicate a need to use a time-dependent reference\nwater correction.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAs a precaution, even if the investigator intends to have high quality purified water at sea, it is wise to\ndetermine the standard water optical density relative to freshly purified water before a cruise, and as a timeseries to understand the quality of the purified water system used for reference.\n\nSoluble Absorption Sample Preparation, Storage and Analysis\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nWash hands with soap and water to avoid contaminating the samples.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nUse 0.2 \xc2\xb5m polycarbonate filters (e.g. Nuclepore or equivalent). Do not use irgalan black stained (low\nfluorescence background) polycarbonate filters for this preparation. Other membrane filters, or Sterivex\ncartridges, may also be used, but the investigator must then test for any contamination by the filter and\nensure that no artifacts are introduced.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe filtration system used should be equipped with control of vacuum for each individual filtration funnel\nand with a provision for direct filtration into clean bottles. An example of a suitable soluble absorption\nfiltration assembly is illustrated in Mitchell et al. (2000).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPre-soak each filter for at least 15 min in 10 % HCl. Rinse the filter thoroughly with purified water.\nMount the filter on a filtration funnel and filter ~100 mL of purified water through it into a sample bottle.\nShake the bottle, and discard the water, pouring it over the inside of the cap to rinse it. Cover the filtration\nfunnel with aluminum foil until ready to filter the sample.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCollect ~200 mL of seawater into a clean sample bottle. For the blanks, use purified water drawn directly\nfrom the purification unit into 2 clean sample bottles.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFilter ~75 mL of the samples and 1 blank directly into clean bottles at low vacuum (<120 mm Hg). Do not\nallow filters to go dry during sample rinsing. Shake the bottles, and discard the water.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFilter ~75 mL of the samples into bottles. For the blank, filter ~75 mL of purified water. When finished,\ncap the bottles and store them until they are to be measured in the spectrophotometer.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIf the samples will be measured within 4 hr, store them in the dark at room temperature.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIf the samples will be measured 4 hr to 24 hr later, refrigerate them in the dark.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nLonger storage is not recommended, because artifacts of undocumented magnitude are known to occur.\nSeveral researchers have reported results from measurements of frozen samples, but no systematic\nevaluation of possible artifacts resulting from freezing has yet been reported.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nWarm refrigerated samples to room temperature before beginning optical density measurements. If it is\npractical to do so, control the samples and the reference water to equal temperatures during the\nspectrophotometric measurements. Absorption by water is strongly temperature dependent at red and near\ninfrared wavelengths (Pegau and Zaneveld 1993).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nQorpak bottles can be re-used at sea. After spectrophotometric analysis is completed, thoroughly rinse\neach bottle and its cap three times with purified water, pour in 20 mL of 10 % HCl acid, and close the cap.\nBefore the bottle is reused, shake it well, discard the 10 % HCl, rinse the bottle and cap copiously with\npurified water, and fill the bottle with purified water, to be used later to rinse a new sample filter. Purified\nwater should be drawn directly from the pure water system.\n\nDetermination of Optical Density of Soluble Absorption Preparations\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIf samples have been refrigerated, allow them to warm to room temperature.\n\n49\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAllow the spectrophotometer to warm up for 30 min. Confirm that the optical windows of the\nspectrophotometer are clean. If necessary, clean them with purified water and ethanol, sequentially, and\ndry them thoroughly with lint-free laboratory tissues.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nVerify the instrument\xe2\x80\x99s spectral characteristics and precision as described in Section 4.3.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nWash hands with soap and water to avoid contamination\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nBetween use, 10 cm quartz window spectrophotometer cuvettes should be stored with purified water. For\nanalysis, discard the purified water in the cuvettes, rinse inside and outside of cuvettes twice with 10 %\nHCl, twice with ethanol, then rinse them inside and outside using copious volumes of purified water. After\nthe cuvettes have been cleaned, use laboratory tissues to handle them. Avoid contacting the cuvettes with\nbare-hands, and do not contaminate their optical windows by touching them.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFill both cuvettes with purified water drawn directly from the water preparation system. Use of purified\nwater stored in containers is not recommended. However, if freshly purified water is not available at sea,\nthe carefully prepared standard water in combusted bottles can be used as a reference, but the investigator\nmust document its degradation over time relative to air (see above).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCarefully dry the cuvettes. Bulk dry with paper towels, but dry the quartz optical windows with lint-free\nlaboratory wipes only (e.g. Kimwipes\xe2\x84\xa2).\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nInspect cuvettes carefully, especially along their optical paths, to ensure that they are clean. Make sure\nthere are no bubbles, floating dust, or contaminants on the optical windows, or in suspension. Looking\nthrough the cuvette against a black background can usually identify any problems in the samples. Repeat\ncleaning and drying procedures as needed to obtain a clean sample.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRun an air\xe2\x80\x93to-air baseline reference spectrum for the spectrophotometer. Record the digital air baseline.\nThis spectrum should be spectrally flat, with noise less than \xc2\xb1 0.0005 OD .\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPlace the reference cuvette in spectrophotometer and scan ODrwa ( \xce\xbb ) , the optical density of purified water\nrelative to air. Remove the reference cuvette and repeat the measurement for the sample cuvette. Store\nboth spectra noting which file is for the cuvette to be used as reference in subsequent analyses, and which is\nto be used for samples. See Figure 4.2 for spectra of ODrwa ( \xce\xbb ) determined during ACE-Asia.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nCompare the spectra of ODrwa ( \xce\xbb ) determined for the reference and sample cuvettes to each other, and\nwith a digital library of previous reference water to air optical density spectra. Ensure that the two cuvettes\nare well matched optically, and that both conform to tolerance of pure water relative to air. Note anomalies\nand plan to make any needed corrections during data processing. If anomalies are associated with poor\npreparation of the cuvette, repeat the preparation and run new water-to-air baseline reference scans.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPut both reference and sample cuvettes filled with purified reference water into the spectrophotometer for a\ndouble beam unit. For a single beam unit this will be done sequentially. Run a baseline correction for\npurified water. After the water-to-water baseline optical density measurement is complete, record the pure\nwater baseline as a sample, ODrww ( \xce\xbb ) . This spectrum should be spectrally flat, with magnitude less than\n\xc2\xb1 0.0005 OD . Save the digital baseline spectrum. Ensure the baseline is flat and stable over time and note\nany anomalies. It is common for the baseline to exhibit temperature-dependent artifacts 650-800 nm.\nThese should be minimized if possible by ensuring the purified water in the sample and reference cuvette\nare at the same temperature.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nIf the baseline reference spectrum ODrww ( \xce\xbb ) is not flat and stable during analysis according to\nspecifications summarized in section 4.3, the precision of any estimate of soluble absorption may be\nseriously questioned. It is the investigator\xe2\x80\x99s responsibility to ensure satisfactory performance of the\ninstruments and use of proper methods to ensure that the final result is reasonable. Significant deviation\nfrom the specifications in section 4.3 or improper consideration of sample preparation protocols may result\nin estimates of soluble absorption that are not meaningful given the small magnitude of this estimate in the\nvisible spectral region of most interest for ocean color applications.\n\n50\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRemove the sample cuvette and discard the liquid. Rinse the inside of the cuvette three times with ~5 mL\nto 10 mL of the next sample to be measured. A copious rinse is desired, but sample volume is often\nlimited. Several vigorously shaken small sample rinses are recommended if the volume is extremely\nlimited.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFill the sample cuvette with the purified water that has been filtered as though it were a sample and record\nthe blank spectrum, OD bs ( \xce\xbb ) , relative to the reference cuvette filled directly from the purified water\nsource..\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nRepeat the rinsing for each subsequent sample. The first sample rinse for seawater samples is most\nimportant to eliminate all purified water, especially for seawater samples due to refractive index differences\nbetween fresh and salt water. Fill the cuvette with the next water sample.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPrior to running each sample, dry the exterior of the sample cuvette carefully, and inspect it, as described\nabove, to ensure a clean sample.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nReplace the sample cuvette in the spectrophotometer, and measure the ODs ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum relative to\nfreshly purified water. Store the digital data and record all necessary information.\n\n4.6 DATA PROCESSING AND ANALYSIS\nThe protocols in this section should be followed to compute particle and soluble material absorption coefficients\nfrom the spectrophotometric OD measurements described above. The following discussion assumes that all\nmeasured OD ( \xce\xbb ) spectra, whether for samples, or reference blanks, have been corrected for the instrument baseline\nspectrum, either automatically, or by post-measurement calculations appropriate to a particular spectrophotometer\nconfiguration (see above in Section 4.3, and specific reference spectrum measurement checks in the protocols of\nSections 4.4 and 4.5).\nComputations for absorption coefficients of particles concentrated on filters, and for materials dissolved in\nwater, differ primarily in the determination of optical pathlength and in the treatment of reference blanks.\nSoluble Absorption Coefficients\nFor soluble absorption, the calculations are directly proportional to the sample optical density relative to the\npure water reference after correction for the pure water blank and specification of a null absorption\n2.303\n\xef\xa3\xae[ODs ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 ODbs ( \xce\xbb )] \xe2\x88\x92 ODnull \xef\xa3\xb9 ,\n(4.3)\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nl \xef\xa3\xb0\nwhere l is the cuvette pathlength (usually 0.1 m), OD s ( \xce\xbb ) is the optical density of the filtrate sample relative to\nag ( \xce\xbb ) =\n\npurified water, OD bs ( \xce\xbb ) is optical density of a purified water blank treated like a sample relative to purified water\n(see below), and ODnull is the apparent residual optical density at a long visible or near infrared wavelength where\nabsorption by dissolved materials is assumed to be zero. Note that as long as the null wavelength region is the same\nfor sample and blank, the sample and blank spectra can be set to zero at the null wavelength independently or after\nthey are subtracted from each other, as indicated in Equation (4.3). Equation (4.3) assumes use of a\nspectrophotometer that automatically references the sample and blank optical density to freshly purified water.\nMost modern commercial single or double beam units will compute this optical density directly relative to the\nreference. The user must record both raw sample and blank optical densities relative to purified water, and assess\nthe stability of the purified water OD rww ( \xce\xbb ) reference by routine determinations of the purified water relative to air\n(e.g. ODrwa ( \xce\xbb ) ; Figure 4.2) and also evaluate the sample preparation methods by determining the blanks routinely\n(e.g. daily when at sea; Figure 3B).\n\n51\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\na. Filtered pure water blank spectra\nThere are generally small spectral effects of the filtration and preparation procedure that cause blanks prepared\nfrom purified water to have a higher OD bs ( \xce\xbb ) at short wavelengths compared to the reference cuvette containing\npurified water drawn directly from the purification system. Examples of filtered blank spectra OD bs ( \xce\xbb ) for ACEAsia where Millipore Alpha-Q water was used as the purified water source in the reference cuvette are illustrated in\nFigures 4.3B and 4.3D (c.f. Mitchell et al. 2000). The OD bs ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum should be determined, recorded and\nincluded with the data for each sample. It is recommended that the investigator carefully determine these blanks for\neach station, or at least once per day, during a field program, and evaluate the stability of this blank for quality\ncontrol purposes. If the purified water system is performing well, and the preparation procedures are carefully\nimplemented, the OD bs ( \xce\xbb ) sample blank offsets will generally be very consistent (Figure 4.3B). In such cases, the\nrecommended procedure is to average OD bs ( \xce\xbb ) spectra over the entire cruise, and to then fit a smoothed exponential\nfunction over wavelength to the overall mean (the bold line in Figure 4.3B). A separate ODnull (see discussion\nbelow) should be determined for the averaged and smoothed OD bs ( \xce\xbb ) spectrum before it is substituted in Equation\n(4.3). Because the signals are small, instrument noise is a large fraction of the signal, even for high quality\nspectrophotometers. Therefore subtraction of an individual blank spectrum, including its noise, is strongly\ndiscouraged as this effectively doubles the noise of an already noisy signal. Instead, it is recommended that a\nsmoothed blank be determined from many individual blank spectra provided that the investigator can demonstrate,\nas in Figure 4.3B that there is consistency among the population of blank spectra that are determined. The\nprocedure of determining blanks at least each day during routine sampling provides an important quality control on\nthe sample preparation protocols. If the blank is found to deviate considerably from the norm, the investigator\nshould immediately determine the cause of the discrepancy.\nb. Null point corrections to soluble absorption spectra\nThe absorption spectrum of pure water varies strongly with temperature, especially in the wavelength region\nbetween 650 nm and 750 nm, but at other wavelengths as well (Pegau and Zaneveld, 1993). To avoid temperature\nrelated measurement artifacts, the sample and reference should be maintained at the same temperature, but in\npractice, this is often difficult to do. If strong temperature residuals are apparent in the spectra near 750 nm, one\nmust inspect the data to determine an appropriate wavelength range to use as a null point. For the data in Figure 4.3,\nit appears that assuming a null point as the average from 590-600 nm is reasonable. This assumption may not be\nreasonable in turbid lake, bay and coastal waters, however, where large soluble absorption values may persist into\nthe near IR. Selection of wavelengths for null correction must be evaluated carefully for each data set, following\nprinciples discussed at more length by Mitchell et al. (2000).\nParticle Absorption Coefficients\nTo compute particle absorption a p ( \xce\xbb ) in suspension from spectrophotometric OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) measured with the\nparticles concentrated on a GF/F filter, it is necessary to appropriately adjust the optical pathlength. This includes\nsubstituting the geometric optical pathlength of the particles in suspension, and a scaling factor, \xce\xb2 , accounting for\nthe increase in the optical measurement path by scattering within the filter sample. The geometric absorption\npathlength ls of the filtered material in suspension is given by\nls =\n\nVf\n,\nAf\n\n(4.4)\n\nwhere Vf is the volume of water filtered and Af is the clearance area of the filter calculated from the diameter Df of\nthe part of each filter that contains the particles. Df should be determined very carefully on numerous individual\nfilters using AN accurate measurement tool like a caliper that is accurate to at least 0.1 mm.\n\n52\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nScattering of light within the GF/F filter increases the absorption pathlength. The absorption coefficient of\nfiltered particles must be corrected for pathlength amplification and the equivalent absorption coefficient in m-1 in\nsuspension is computed as\nap (\xce\xbb ) =\n\n2.303 A f\n\xef\xa3\xae[OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 OD bf ( \xce\xbb )] \xe2\x88\x92 OD null \xef\xa3\xb9 ,\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\xce\xb2Vf \xef\xa3\xb0\n\n(4.5)\n\nwhere OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) is the measured optical density of the sample filter, OD bf ( \xce\xbb ) is the optical density of a fully\nhydrated blank filter, and OD null is a null wavelength residual correction from the infrared where particle\nabsorption is minimal. See also detailed discussion of null point selection in Mitchell et al. (2000)\na. Particle absorption blank spectra\nIf a spectrophotometer with automatic reference baseline correction is used, and the reference filter blank\nbaseline is flat over the spectral range of interest, OD bf ( \xce\xbb ) does not need to be subtracted. Spectra of OD bf ( \xce\xbb )\nmust be determined, recorded and provided with the sample data. Properly prepared blanks generally have flat\nspectra relative to the reference baseline filters. If the OD bf ( \xce\xbb ) is confirmed to be flat, then it is recommended that\nonly a null absorbance is subtracted from the OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) to compensate for baseline offsets. Subtraction of a\nspectrally flat baseline that varies only due to the instrument noise increases the noise of the result. If the instrument\nbaseline cannot be maintained within the recommendations summarized in Section 4.3, the investigator should\nconsider using a different instrument since the errors in the methods caused by using unstable instruments are\ndifficult to control for.\nb. Null point corrections to particle absorption spectra\nTo correct for residual offsets in the sample filter relative to the reference, and for scattering artifacts due to\nparticle loading, it is assumed that a null absorption wavelength in the infrared can be identified. Historically, many\ninvestigators used 750 nm as the null absorption wavelength, but recent reports indicate that this wavelength is too\nshort for some waters. It is recommended that the null wavelength be set at 800 nm (or longer), and that the\ninvestigator must examine the spectra to evaluate residual absorption structure near the null wavelength. Rather\nthan use a single wavelength, a mean OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) in a 10 nm interval (e.g. 790 nm to 800 nm) may be used as the null\nvalue to minimize the introduction of noise in the null correction procedure. Mitchell et al. (2000) discuss, at more\nlength, factors affecting the choice of an appropriate wavelength for estimating OD null . In Case 2 waters, the\ndefinition of the null absorption is more difficult and the investigator may consider the benefits of the transmissionreflectance estimates of particle absorption (Tassan and Ferrari, 1995a).\nc. Pathlength amplification corrections\nTo correct for the pathlength increases due to multiple scattering in the filter, the prevalent current practice is to\nestimate \xce\xb2 empirically through either a quadratic or power function that may be expressed in the form\n\xe2\x88\x921\n\n\xce\xb2 = \xef\xa3\xaeC1 + C2 \xef\xa3\xaeOD fp ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 ODnull ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9 \xef\xa3\xb9 ,\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\xef\xa3\xbb\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\n(4.6a)\n\nor\n\xce\xb2 = C0 + C1 \xef\xa3\xaeOD fp ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 ODnull ( \xce\xbb ) \xef\xa3\xb9\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\nC2\n\n,\n\n(4.6b)\n\nfor quadratic equation or power function fits, respectively. C0, C1 and C2 are coefficients of least squares regression\nfits of measured data. Recommended coefficients have been reported in the literature (Table 1). The investigator\nshould either choose published coefficients consistent with the species composition, equipment and measurement\nconditions for a given data set (consider the discussion in Mitchell et al. 2000), or independently determine\n\n53\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\npathlength amplification factors by comparing absorption in suspension and on filters following procedures\ndescribed previously (Mitchell 1990, Mitchell et al., 2000).\nd. De-pigmented Particle and Phytoplankton Absorption Coefficients\nThe de-pigmented particle absorption coefficients, ad ( \xce\xbb ) , may be calculated using Equation (4.5), by\nsubstituting. OD fd ( \xce\xbb ) for OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) . At present it is recommended to use the same pathlength correction factor for\nthe de-pigmented samples as for the particle absorption sample. The validity of this operational choice of \xce\xb2 is\ndifficult to assess, because the de-pigmented particles are created operationally from the treatment, and the\nrelationships between their absorption on filters compared to suspensions may differ from those derived empirically\nfor the original particles.\nThe spectral absorption coefficient for phytoplankton pigments can be computed as the difference between\nparticulate and de-pigmented estimates:\na\xcf\x86 ( \xce\xbb ) = a p ( \xce\xbb ) \xe2\x88\x92 ad ( \xce\xbb ) .\n\n(4.7)\n\n4.7 DATA REPORTING\nFor purposes of data reporting and archiving, the absorption coefficients will be reported in m\xe2\x80\x931 and computed\nusing the equations summarized above. Uncorrected optical density spectra for the filter samples, blank filter\nreferenced to a blank filter, pure water referenced to air, pure water referenced to pure water and soluble absorption\nblank spectra must be recorded and provided so alternative algorithms could be applied to the original data. The\npathlength amplification factor, a description of (or reference to) the method and the procedure for assignment of the\nnull absorption, and any blank or spectral scattering corrections for the soluble absorption calculations must be\nreported.\n\n4.9 PROTOCOL STATUS AND FUTURE DIRECTIONS\nAbsorption spectra for particles filtered on GF/F filters\nDetails of various issues related to this frequently used method for estimating particle absorption for filtered\nsamples are not significantly changed since the summary of the NASA-sponsored Workshops found in Mitchell et\nal. (2000). It is important to address a few salient issues that are routinely asked by investigators interested in\nimplementing the method. First, most modern dual-beam spectrophotometers that have a grating before the sample\nand illuminate the sample with spectrally resolved light have negligible differences (a few percent) in terms of\ndetermining the raw GF/F filter OD fp ( \xce\xbb ) of the particles relative to a properly hydrated blank filter if the protocols\nare carefully followed. Thus, it is not essential to determine the pathlength amplification factor, \xce\xb2 , for each different\nspectrophotometer that is used as long as the investigator makes an appropriate choice of instrumentation.\nHowever, some spectrophotometers have limited spectral range, limited dynamic range, more noise and inferior\nstability so the investigator should evaluate the unit to be used to ensure suitability by following the\nrecommendations in section 4.3. Second, diode-array systems that illuminate with broad-band light and then\ndisperse the post-sample light using a spectral photodiode array may have significantly different raw OD for the\nfiltered sample. Example OD spectra estimated for a diatom culture for various systems used at the Scripps\nWorkshop are shown in Figure 4.1 (see also details in Mitchell et al. 2000). Note the Hewlett-Packard spectral\ndiode array system has a significantly higher OD than the other instruments. An empirical relation for this offset in\nthe range 400-700 nm is reported in Mitchell et al. (2000) for that specific model. The Hewlett-Packard data is\nreported only for wavelengths greater than 400 nm because the instrument performs poorly at short wavelengths\nwith the glass fiber filter method. If a user chooses such optical geometry for the determination of particle\nabsorption they should carefully assess the potential issues illustrated in Figure 4.1. We recommend that the user\ncompare several spectrophotometers for raw optical density of properly hydrated samples relative to blank filters\nand ensure the unit they use does not deviate from the typical result of most systems for which amplifications factors\n(\xce\xb2) have been determined (Table 4.1). Alternatively one must determine the pathlength amplification for the\n\n54\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\ninstrument of choice, a laborious and unnecessary procedure if a spectrophotometer is selected that does not cause\nthe bias illustrated in Figure 4.1.\nAbsorption spectra for particles transferred to glass slides\nAn alternative method, developed by Allali et al. (1995), to estimate absorption coefficients of cultures and\nseawater samples is to freeze transfer the particles to transparent microscope slides, following the protocols of\nHewes and Holm-Hansen (1983). The investigator must have an integrating sphere or equivalent scattered\ntransmission accessory to implement this method. This procedure produced results comparable to the GF/F filter\nmethod in comparisons reported by Mitchell et al. (2000), but sufficient uncertainties remain that the GF/F method\ncontinues to be recommended for the present.\nTransmission-Reflectance (T-R) Method\nTassan and Ferrari (1995a) described a modification of the light-transmission method that corrects for\nbackscattering. This technique combines light-transmission (T) and light-reflection (R) measurements, carried out\nusing an integrating sphere attached to a dual-beam spectrophotometer. The data analysis is performed by a\ntheoretical model that eliminates the effect of light backscattering by the particles. At the Scripps workshop, the\nglobal error of the T-R method was comparable to the error yielded by the simpler T method for monocultures.\nSubsequent modifications of the T-R experimental routine (Tassan and Ferrari, 1998; Ferrari and Tassan, 1999)\nyielded a significant reduction of the experimental error. Tassan and Ferrari (1995) reported that for case 1 waters\nthat have negligible inorganic particle load, the amplification factor for GF/F filters determined with the T-R\nmethods is similar to those determined by Mitchell (1990). The T-R method is particularly suited for applications to\nsamples containing highly scattering mineral particles that are commonly found in Case 2 waters. Despite the more\ncomplicated procedure including an instrument with an integrating sphere, this method should be considered in\nspecial circumstances, and with further development may eventually supercede the presently recommended\ntransmission protocol.\nAbsorption spectra for seawater filtered through membrane filters or cartridges\nFor most ocean regions, the optical density of dissolved organic material, relative to purified water in a typical\n10 cm pathlength cuvette, is very small in the 400-600 nm region of most interest to ocean color satellite\ninvestigations. To ensure a common frame of reference for the global data collected by diverse investigators, we\nrecommend ODrwa ( \xce\xbb ) spectra (250-850 nm) be determined relative to air for purified water directly introduced to\nproperly cleaned quartz cuvettes. The purpose of such spectra is to obtain an independent reference of the quality of\nthe purified water. ODrwa ( \xce\xbb ) spectra for the sample cuvette used during ACE-Asia are shown in Figure 4.2.\nODrwa ( \xce\xbb ) should be determined daily for the sample and reference cuvettes used in analyses. The investigator\n\nshould keep careful records of this data and assess any bias in final estimates that may be attributed to problems with\nthe reference water. By plotting in the range of minimal absorption by water (250-600 nm; Figure 4.2A) one can\nassess whether or not the reference water on a ship has seriously degraded. Production of impure water by\ncommercial systems is a relatively common problem on ships where the feed water may have serious contamination.\nIf the purified water system fails at sea, the investigator should use the standard water prepared prior to the cruise as\nthe reference. Spectra of ODrwa ( \xce\xbb ) of the bottled standard water should be determined before and after a cruise for\neach lot of bottled standards that are prepared. This precaution is important to assist in any corrections that might be\nrequired if standard water is used as a reference, or if the purified water system degrades over time during a cruise.\nThere are still relatively few spectra of soluble absorption determined fresh at sea using the revised protocols\nrecommended here. Spectra of ODs ( \xce\xbb ) and OD bs ( \xce\xbb ) collected during ACE-Asia are shown in Figure 4.3. Raw\noptical density, relative to Millipore Alpha-Q water are shown in 3A. We routinely find small positive offsets from\n600-800 nm that we feel should be compensated by subtracting a null value. Figure 4.3B illustrates OD bs ( \xce\xbb ) during\nACE-Asia prepared as recommended in section 4.5, but plotted at 10x smaller scale as Figure 4.3A. The\nrecommended procedure is to subtract a cruise (or global) mean of this blank (solid line in Figure 4.3B) from the\nraw sample OD values, and then to adjust this difference to zero at a null reference [Equation (4.3)]. The smoothed\n\n55\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nglobal blank was determined by taking the mean of all blanks for each cruise we have completed since 1998,\nsubsequently taking the mean of all cruises and lastly fitting an exponential function to the global mean after setting\na null point as the average from 590-600 nm. There can be small differences in blank spectra cruise to cruise, but\nwe do not find this to be significant relative to the overall statistics of all cruises or the variance within a single\ncruse. For relatively weakly absorbing samples like open oceans observed during ACE-Asia, there is negligible\napparent absorption > 600 nm and there is clear evidence in 3A of uncompensated temperature effects 650-800 nm.\nTherefore we chose to set the null value as the mean from 590-600 nm. However, if very strong soluble absorption\nis present, the temperature effects 650-800 nm will be less significant, and the absorption 590-600 nm may be\nimportant. The investigator should evaluate their data to determine the best null point and report that assessment.\nFigure 4.3C are optical density of spectra for a 10 cm cuvette after correcting for the null value and the blank\nspectrum The effort to carefully determine the purified water relative to air, and blanks during each cruise will\nallow different investigators to inter-compare their results better, and will ensure better quality control of data\ncollected over time. We have also determined the time-dependent change of our standard water (data not shown),\nand when we use that as a reference due to the failure of our purified water system at sea, we subtract a different\nblank than the global fit shown in Figure 4.3B.\nAn alternate method for preparing samples for soluble absorption allows multiple use of Sterivex sealed\nfiltration cartridges. Use of these cartridges has been described by D\xe2\x80\x99Sa et al. (1999) who used the method to\nprepare samples delivered to a capillary light guide spectrophotometer for estimating absorption by soluble material.\nThe procedure provides high sensitivity and can be adapted to continuous flow determinations. This new method\nmay prove useful in various applications but has not been applied extensively at this time. Evaluation of the\nperformance of the Sterivex cartridges for sample preparation, and of light guides for spectroscopy, warrant further\nresearch.\nConstraints on the estimate of soluble and particle absorption\nTo constrain our water sample estimates of particle and soluble absorption we have compared them to spectral\nestimates of the diffuse attenuation coefficient for downwelling irradiance, K d ( z , \xce\xbb ) , determined using a free-fall\nradiometer during a Southern Ocean cruise (AMLR) and a western Pacific Ocean cruise (ACE-Asia). It is well\nknown that accurate estimate of K d ( z , \xce\xbb ) in the upper ocean is difficult. Problems include heave of the ship, foam,\nbubbles, shadow, tilt, sky conditions and other influences on this apparent optical property (see more detailed\ndiscussions in other chapters of these protocols). Waters et al. (1990) described advantages of free-fall systems and\nmany investigators have adopted this procedure to minimize some of the problems cited above. In 2001 we\ndeployed our Biospherical Instruments PRR 800 system at approximately 80 stations combined between our AMLR\nand ACE-Asia cruises. We consider this our highest quality radiometric data set because of the free-fall\ndeployment, the spectral range from 312-710 nm and because we acquired 4-5 separate free-fall profiles at each\nstation to improve the confidence in our final estimate. In Figure 4.4 we show estimates of the mean cosine for\nspectral downwelling irradiance, \xc2\xb5 d ( \xce\xbb ) , of the upper ocean mixed layer (open symbols). Here we define \xc2\xb5 d ( \xce\xbb ) as\nthe ratio [aw ( \xce\xbb ) + ap ( \xce\xbb ) + ag ( \xce\xbb )] / K d . For Figure 4.4, values for pure water are estimated from Pope and Fry\n(1997) for 380-700 nm, Quickenden and Irvin (1980) for 300-320 and a linear interpolation between those values for\n320-380 nm as recommended by Fry (2000). If the individual components are accurate, this can be considered a\nreasonable estimate of the mean cosine near the ocean surface (see Mobley, 1994 for detailed discussion of the mean\ncosine). Theoretically the values of \xc2\xb5 d ( \xce\xbb ) should be less than 1.0 and for typical radiance distributions of the upper\nocean, they should be in the range of 0.70-0.85 near the surface. For both AMLR and ACE-Asia all absorption data\nwere determined fresh at sea with consistent methods between the two cruises. We found that in the region 500 nm\nto 650 nm there is little difference between the estimates of \xc2\xb5 d ( \xce\xbb ) for the Southern Ocean and the western Pacific.\nHowever, below 500 nm, the values for ACE-Asia are near 1.0 and below 400 nm they exceed 1.0. For AMLR,\nvalues approach 1.0 for wavelengths less than 350 nm.\nThe ratio of ag ( \xce\xbb ) / at ( \xce\xbb ) where at = aw + ap + ag , is also plotted in Figure 4.4 (filled symbols).\n\nThe trend\n\nclearly illustrates that the soluble component dominates at short wavelength. There are several hypotheses that\nshould be considered to understand the overestimates of \xc2\xb5 d ( \xce\xbb ) below 400 nm. These could include underestimate\nof K d ( z , \xce\xbb ) or overestimates of any of the absorption components. A combination of these factors may prevail.\n\n56\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nThe filter radiometer in the profiler has good out of band blocking, but the spectrum of surface irradiance is rapidly\nchanging in the region <350 nm and this may cause a red shift in the effective band center of the channels, with an\nassociated underestimate of K d ( z , \xce\xbb ) . There may be small particles or colloids that pass the 0.2 \xc2\xb5m filters causing\na spectrally dependent scattering error (Aas, 2000). The particle absorption we estimate is based on Mitchell (1990),\nwhich results in higher estimates compared to some other published methods (Table 1). Also, there has not been\nadequate attention paid to determination of the pathlength amplification factor for the region below 400 nm. It is\nalso possible that the values for pure water absorption are too high. The very reasonable or slightly high (by about\n10-15%) values for the mean cosine of downwelling irradiance shown in Figure 4.4 for 400-600 nm indicates that\nthe absorption methods recommended here are rather robust compared to simple estimates of diffuse attenuation\ncoefficients. Reynolds et al. (2001) and Stramska et al. (2000) have reported reasonable closure between estimates\nof absorption using these methods, radiometric observations and modeling.\nWe have used \xe2\x80\x9cpure water\xe2\x80\x9d absorption for our estimate of aw ( \xce\xbb ) , and salts should in fact be added, if\nimportant, in the comparison of absorption to diffuse attenuation in Figure 4.4. Our estimate of ag ( \xce\xbb ) relative to\npurified water will include absorption by salts, if they are significant. Salts in seawater are significant absorbers at\nshort wavelengths. Lenoble (1956; see also Shiffrin, 1988) reported values for pure salts dissolved in purified water\nthat indicate absorption coefficients near 300 nm comparable to the sample optical density of filtered samples\nrelative to purified water that we routinely determine at sea in this spectral region. This UV absorption (<320 nm),\nrelative to purified water, is generally assumed to be caused by \xe2\x80\x9ccolored dissolved organic matter\xe2\x80\x9d but this may be\ninaccurate at these short wavelengths. Therefore one must be very cautious interpreting the apparent optical density\nof seawater filtrates relative to purified water for wavelengths less than 320nm. We recommend that more careful\nresearch should be carried out on the methods for soluble absorption, which appears to have a potentially\ndominating influence on the overestimates of \xc2\xb5 d ( \xce\xbb ) less than 400 nm. In particular, the influence of scattering by\nsmall particles (organic or mineral) and the role of salt absorption must be more carefully assessed.\n\nREFERENCES\nAas, E., 2000: Spectral slope of yellow substance: problems caused by small particles. Ronald V. Zaneveld1, Scott Pegau1 and James L. Mueller2\n1\nCollege of Oceanographic and Atmospheric Sciences, Oregon State University, Corvallis\n2\nCenter for Hydro-Optics and Remote Sensing, San Diego State University, California\n\n5.1 INTRODUCTION\n-1 -1\n\n-1\n\nThe volume scattering function (VSF) \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) [sr m ] and the volume absorption coefficient a ( \xce\xbb ) [m ]\nprovide the most fundamental description of a medium\xe2\x80\x99s inherent optical properties (IOP), as all other IOP can be\nderived from them. In particular, the volume scattering coefficient b ( \xce\xbb ) and volume backscattering coefficient\nbb ( \xce\xbb ) may be derived by integrating \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) over the unit sphere and backward hemisphere, respectively. In\n\nterms of determining the \xe2\x80\x9ccomplete\xe2\x80\x9d IOP from in situ measurements, a useful combination is a ( \xce\xbb ) , the beam\nattenuation coefficient c ( \xce\xbb ) , and the volume scattering phase function \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) =\n\n\xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 )\n\nc (\xce\xbb) \xe2\x88\x92 a (\xce\xbb)\n\n. The reader is\n\nreferred to Vol. I, Chapter 2 (Sect. 2.4) and to Chapter 1 of the present volume for further details regarding these\ndefinitions and relationships.\nKnowledge of the VSF is a critical prerequisite to accurate radiative transfer modeling of remote sensing\nreflectance and water-leaving radiance. In Vol. III, Chapter 4 (and references cited there), it is shown that irradiance\nreflectance R ( \xce\xbb ) is approximately proportional to the ratio of the backscattering to absorption coefficients, and that\nupwelled radiance just beneath the sea surface is proportional to R ( \xce\xbb ) , so that\nLu ( 0\xe2\x88\x92 , \xce\xbb, \xce\xb8, \xcf\x86 ) = Ed ( 0\xe2\x88\x92 , \xce\xbb )\n\nf bb ( \xce\xbb )\n.\nQ a (\xce\xbb)\n\nThis relationship is completely general and exact, but it does not express a linear proportionality to the IOP ratio,\nbecause the factors f and Q are not simply coefficients. .They are functions, f \xef\xa3\xae \xce\xbb, ( \xce\xb8 o , \xcf\x84 a ,W ) , a ( \xce\xbb ) , \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xce\xa8 ) \xef\xa3\xb9 and\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nQ \xef\xa3\xae( \xce\xbb, \xce\xb8\xe2\x80\xb2, \xcf\x86 ) , ( \xce\xb8 o , \xcf\x84 a , W ) , a ( \xce\xbb ) , \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xce\xa8 ) \xef\xa3\xb9 , that account for the bidirectional nature of the ocean\xe2\x80\x99s reflectance; this\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\nbidirectionality may be traced directly to the shape of the VSF. Bidirectionality of remote sensing reflectance, as it\narises from the VSF, is shown more explicitly in the formulation by Zaneveld (1982).\nEquation (1.18) could be used directly as a basis for measuring the VSF if an instrument\xe2\x80\x99s source and detector\nwere well collimated, and there were no flux losses, or FOV distortions, associated with an its optical elements.\nSome general angle scattering meters (Petzold 1972; Lee et al. 2003) are designed with a well-enough collimated\nbeam and very small detector acceptance angle, so that equation (1.18) may be applied directly. In terms of\nequation (1.19), this is equivalent to assuming that the scattering response weighting function W ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95; c ) is\nnarrow enough to set it to unity. The General Angle Scattering Meter (GASM), built at the Scripps Institution of\nOceanography\xe2\x80\x99s Visibility Laboratory (Petzold 1972), consists of a lamp focused into a cylindrical beam, and a\nnarrow field of view detector mounted to swing in an arc to view the beam at many off-axis scattering angles\nbetween approximately 10o and 170o. Petzold (1972) reported VSF\xe2\x80\x99s measured for selected natural waters using the\n\n65\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\n(GASM). This reference has been widely used to describe shapes of \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) in natural waters. Mobley et al. (2001)\ncompared measured and modeled nadir-viewing remote sensing reflectances, using measured c ( \xce\xbb ) and a ( \xce\xbb ) with\n\xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) of different assumed shapes, including that of Petzold (1972), and the VSF measured using a general angle\n\nscattering meter of a new design (Lee et al., 2003); best agreement was achieved using the measured VSF. Their\nresults showed that large systematic offsets can result if one arbitrarily assumes a scattering phase function having\nan incorrect backward scattering fraction. The results of Mobley et al. (2001) also indicated that nadir-viewing\nradiance reflectance is less sensitive to the detailed shape of the forward scattering lobe of \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) . Details of\nscattering at intermediate forward angles might, however, be more important for off-nadir viewing geometry (Vol.\nIII, Ch. 4).\nAt present, commercially available VSF sensors12 are designed with detector beam-spread and detector\nacceptance angles ranging from 10o to 20o Full-Width Half-Maximum (FWHM). These instruments measure the\nVSF, weighted as in (1.19), at one (or a few) scattering angle(s) and are typically used to determine the\nbackscattering coefficient using the methods described below in Sect. 5.4. Although these methods depend on\nassumptions concerning \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) , determinations of bb ( \xce\xbb ) from VSF profile measurements with these instruments,\nwhen combined with absorption and beam attenuation coefficients, radiometric profiles and pigment concentration\nmeasurements, have provide useful information about relationships between IOP, AOP and optically important\nmaterial constituents of the water column (e.g. Stramska et al. 2000).\n\nFig. 5.1: Schematic illustration of a VSF sensor geometry used for numerical integration over\nthe volume intersected by a source beam and detector FOV of incremental elements of the\nscattered radiant flux received by the detector.\n\n12\n\nCertain commercial equipment, instruments, or materials are identified in this chapter to foster understanding.\nSuch identification does not imply recommendation, or endorsement, by the National Aeronautics and Space\nAdministration, nor does it imply that the materials or equipment identified are necessarily the best available for the\npurpose.\n66\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\nAn example of a generalized source and detector arrangement to measure the VSF \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) at a scattering angle\n\xcf\x80\nis illustrated schematically in Fig. 5.1. To determine a calibration factor relating the detector response to\n2\nflux scattered from the source beam into the field of view (FOV) of the detector, we must determine the sensor\xe2\x80\x99s\nresponse weighting function W ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95; c ) [equation (1.19)], either explicitly, or implicitly. The explicit approach\n\xcf\x88>\n\nto this problem is to determine W ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88, \xcf\x95; c ) from geometry and first principles, and then calibrate the instrument\xe2\x80\x99s\nresponse signals in a medium having a known VSF \xce\xb2 ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) ; the detailed steps in this approach are described below\nin Sect. 5.2. The implicit approach (Maffione and Dana 1997) is to insert a plaque of known near-Lambertian\nreflectance in the position of the horizontal xy-plane, at distance z from the source-detector axis, illustrated Fig. 5.1,\nand record the instrument\xe2\x80\x99s responses as the plaque is moved vertically in small increments through the volume\ndefined by the intersection of the source beam and detector FOV; this approach is briefly outlined in Sect. 5.3\nbelow, but the reader is referred to Maffione and Dana (1997) for the detailed derivation and method of\nimplementation.\nFor brevity, we will henceforth omit the explicit wavelength dependence of variables.\n\n5.2 CHARACTERIZATION AND CALIBRATION OF A VSF SENSOR\nFROM ITS GEOMETRY AND RESPONSE TO SCATTERING BY\nPOLYSTYRENE BEADS\nGeometric Determination of W(\xcf\x88)\nFigure 5.1 illustrates a source located at the origin xo = (0, 0, 0) and a detector located on the y-axis at position\nxd = (0, y d , 0) . These particular, triangular source functions were\nderived assuming that both the source beam and detector FOV are conical about the respective centerlines illustrated\nin Fig. 5.1.\nGeneralized Weighting Functions for Arbitrary Source Beam and Detector FOV Geometries\nIn the above development of equations (5.1) through (5.7), it was assumed that the flux emitted by the source\nwas uniformly distributed over a conical beam with its axis directed in direction\n\xef\xa3\xae \xef\xa3\xab\xcf\x80\n\xef\xa3\xb6\xef\xa3\xb9\n\xef\xa3\xaf sin \xef\xa3\xac 2 + \xce\xb8o \xef\xa3\xb7 \xef\xa3\xba\n\xef\xa3\xb8\xef\xa3\xba\n\xef\xa3\xaf \xef\xa3\xad\n\xef\xa3\xaf\n\xef\xa3\xba\n\xcb\x86\n0\n\xce\xb5o = \xef\xa3\xaf\n\xef\xa3\xba,\n\xef\xa3\xaf\n\xef\xa3\xba\n\xef\xa3\xafcos \xef\xa3\xab \xcf\x80 + \xce\xb8 \xef\xa3\xb6 \xef\xa3\xba\no \xef\xa3\xb7\xef\xa3\xba\n\xef\xa3\xaf \xef\xa3\xac2\n\xef\xa3\xb8\xef\xa3\xbb\n\xef\xa3\xb0 \xef\xa3\xad\nand that the detector flux responsivity was also uniformly distributed over a conical FOV having its axis aligned in\ndirection\n\xef\xa3\xae\n\xef\xa3\xab\xcf\x80\n\xef\xa3\xb6\xef\xa3\xb9\n\xef\xa3\xaf \xe2\x88\x92 sin \xef\xa3\xac 2 + \xce\xb8d \xef\xa3\xb7 \xef\xa3\xba\n\xef\xa3\xad\n\xef\xa3\xb8\xef\xa3\xba\n\xef\xa3\xaf\n\xef\xa3\xaf\n\xef\xa3\xba\n\xcb\x86\n0\n\xce\xb5d = \xef\xa3\xaf\n\xef\xa3\xba.\n\xef\xa3\xaf\n\xef\xa3\xba\n\xef\xa3\xaf cos \xef\xa3\xab \xcf\x80 + \xce\xb8 \xef\xa3\xb6 \xef\xa3\xba\nd \xef\xa3\xb7 \xef\xa3\xba\n\xef\xa3\xac\n\xef\xa3\xaf\n\xef\xa3\xad2\n\xef\xa3\xb8\xef\xa3\xbb\n\xef\xa3\xb0\nWe may generalize the vector solid angle associated with the source beam as \xe2\x84\xa6o , which represents the angular solid\n\xcb\x86\nangle domain, measured relative to \xce\xb5 o , over which flux emitted by the source is non-zero. Similarly, \xe2\x84\xa6d is the\n\xcb\x86\nsolid angle domain relative to \xce\xb5 d over which the detector\xe2\x80\x99s responsivity is non-zero. There are no restrictions on\n\neither the patterns of angular limits associated with \xe2\x84\xa6o and \xe2\x84\xa6d , or on the relative distributions of flux within \xe2\x84\xa6o ,\nor of flux responsivity within \xe2\x84\xa6d .\n\xcb\x86\nReal sources generally emit flux in a somewhat irregular beam pattern that typically peaks in the vicinity of \xce\xb5 o ,\nmay be relatively flat for much of its FWHM beam width, and then tends to fall sharply near the edges of the beam\nand gradually decay beyond the nominal FWHM limits. Detector angular responsivity FOV patterns generally\n\xcb\x86\nexhibit similar geometric characteristics relative to \xce\xb5 d .\n\nThe flux variations within the source beam may be expressed as a normalized relative flux distribution function\nho \xef\xa3\xae\xce\xb5 \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6o \xef\xa3\xb9 that takes non-zero values only in directions included in the solid angle beam pattern. Similarly, the\n\xef\xa3\xb0\xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nangular response function of the the detector may be written hd \xef\xa3\xae\xce\xb5 \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6d \xef\xa3\xb9 . To generalize the weighting functions to\n\xef\xa3\xb0\xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xbb\naccount for arbitrary source beam and detector FOV geometries, and relate the phase function to incremental\ndetector responses \xe2\x88\x86Dd \xef\xa3\xae \xe2\x88\x86V ( x ) ; c \xef\xa3\xb9 :\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n1.\n\nMultiply the right-hand-sides of equations (5.1) and (5.2) by ho \xef\xa3\xae\xce\xb5 ( x ) \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6o \xef\xa3\xb9 , and carry this factor\n\xef\xa3\xb0\xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nthrough subsequent substitutions.\n\n69\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\n\xef\xa3\xae \xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xb9\nSubstitute using the relationship \xe2\x88\x86Dd \xef\xa3\xae \xe2\x88\x86V ( x ) ; c \xef\xa3\xb9 = hd \xef\xa3\xb0 \xe2\x88\x92\xce\xb5 ( x d \xe2\x88\x92 x ) \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6d \xef\xa3\xbb \xe2\x88\x86\xce\xa6 d \xef\xa3\xae \xe2\x88\x86V ( x ) ; c \xef\xa3\xb9 on the left\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\n2.\n\nhand-side of (5.7) to relate \xce\xb2 \xef\xa3\xae\xcf\x88 ( x ) \xef\xa3\xb9 to the incremental detector response.\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nAbsorb the source flux and detector responsivity distributions into the weighting function W ( x; c ) by\n\n3.\n\nmultiplying the right-hand-side of (5.7) by the product ho \xef\xa3\xae\xce\xb5 ( x ) \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6o \xef\xa3\xb9 hd \xef\xa3\xae \xe2\x88\x92\xce\xb5 ( xd \xe2\x88\x92 x ) \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6d \xef\xa3\xb9 .\n\xef\xa3\xb0\xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xbb \xef\xa3\xb0 \xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nThe numerical implementation of the general form of (5.7) is straightforward. The more difficult aspect of\nobtaining generalized weighting functions is the experimental determination of the functions ho \xef\xa3\xae\xce\xb5 \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6o \xef\xa3\xb9 and\n\xef\xa3\xb0\xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nhd \xef\xa3\xae\xce\xb5 \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6d \xef\xa3\xb9 .\n\xef\xa3\xb0\xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\nThe detector FOV responsivity distribution function may be measured by mounting the detector in a rotating\nstage, with its entrance aperture centered on the intersection of the axis of rotation and the optical axis of a stable\ncollimated source. The stage is rotated in small angular increments, and the detector response is recorded at each\n\xcb\x86\nangle to measure the relative variations in response in one plane containing \xce\xb5 d . The instrument is then rotated in\n\xcb\x86\nincrements about \xce\xb5 d and the process is repeated to map the distribution of responses in a sequence of planes\nadequate to resolve the full function hd \xef\xa3\xae\xce\xb5 \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6d \xef\xa3\xb9 . This is essentially the same procedure used to characterize the\n\xef\xa3\xb0\xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nangular FOV of field radiometers (Vol. II, Ch. 3).\nThe inverse of the above setup may be used to map the flux distribution of the source beam. The instrument is\nmounted with the source aligned with the optical and rotation axes, and a narrow FOV detector is substituted for the\ncollimated source. The stage is rotated through a suitable angular range to map out flux variations in a plane\n\xcb\x86\n\xcb\x86\nthrough \xce\xb5 o , and the instrument is rotated about \xce\xb5 o and the process repeated to measure flux distributions in a\nsequence of planes. If the temporal stability of flux output by the source is in question, a second detector may be\n\xcb\x86\nmounted to monitor the source output from a fixed off-axis direction relative to \xce\xb5 o , i.e. by mounting it on an\nextension to the rotational stage.\nWhether one must go to the trouble to determine the beam and FOV weighting functions more accurately than\ncan be modeled from simple geometric elements of the optical design depends on the shape of the VSF in the region\nto be measured. The detailed behaviour of the outer edges of the two distribution functions are not important factors\nin determing the weighting functions for measurements of the VSF of pure water, or of a particulate VSF at\nscattering angles near, or greater than, 90o (Figs. 1.2 and 1.3). Therefore, relatively simple approximations to\nho \xef\xa3\xae\xce\xb5 \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6o \xef\xa3\xb9 and hd \xef\xa3\xae\xce\xb5 \xe2\x88\x88 \xe2\x84\xa6d \xef\xa3\xb9 should be completely adequate for the commercial backscattering instruments\n\xef\xa3\xb0\xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\xef\xa3\xb0\xcb\x86\n\xef\xa3\xbb\nmentioned above. Conversely, the angular breadth and details of the beam and FOV distribution functions both\nbecome increasingly more critical when one wishes to measure the VSF at decreasing angles in the forward\ndirection.\nDependence of the Weighting Functions on the Beam Attenuation Coefficient c\nThe effect of the beam attenuation coefficient on the weighting function W ( x; c ) for each volume element is\nexplicitly represented in (5.7) by the transmittance over the combined pathlength from the source to the volume\nelement at x and from there to the source location. The transmittance term is integrated into each angular\nweighting function W ( \xcf\x88; c ) determined using (5.8). Strictly speaking, near-forward scattered light still enters\n\xe2\x88\x86V ( x ) and can be scattered to the detector (see also the discussion of transmissometer acceptance angles in\n\nChapter 2), but we will neglect this complication in the present discussion. The ratios of integrated weighting\n\xe2\x88\xab W ( \xcf\x88; c ) sin \xcf\x88d \xcf\x88 are compared against increasing beam attenuation coefficient c for the three ECOfunctions\n\xe2\x88\xab W ( \xcf\x88; c = 0 ) sin \xcf\x88d \xcf\x88\nVSF weighting functions illustrated in Fig. 5.2. These functions are clearly log-linear, and should we choose to\nrepresent the weighting function exclusively using W ( \xcf\x88; c = 0 ) , it would be a simple matter to adjust the results for\nc dependence.\n\nThis dependence may either be applied as a correction to the weighting function, or by a\n\n70\n\nOcean Optics Protocols For Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4, Volume IV\n\ntransmittance function of the form \xce\xb2p ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88; c ) = \xce\xb2p ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) exp \xef\xa3\xae creff ( \xcf\x88 ) \xef\xa3\xb9 , where \xce\xb2p ( \xce\xbb, \xcf\x88 ) is the measured VSF\n\xef\xa3\xb0\n\xef\xa3\xbb\n\nusing W ( \xcf\x88; c = 0 ) (see below) and reff ( \xcf\x88 ) is an effective net pathlength for the particular scattering angle\n\nmeasurement.\n\nFig. 5.3: Dependence of the integrated weighting functions\n\n\xe2\x88\xab W ( \xcf\x88; c ) sin \xcf\x88d \xcf\x88\n\non the\n\nbeam attenuation coefficient for the 3 ECO-VSF weighting functions of Fig. 5.2.\nCalibration with Polystyrene Spheres\nIn this approach, a sample of polystyrene microsphere beads must be obtained and prepared for use in the\ncalibration procedure. ABSTRACT (Maximum 200 words)\n\nThis document stipulates protocols for measuring bio-optical and radiometric data for the Sensor\nIntercomparison and Merger for Biological and Interdisciplinary Oceanic Studies (SIMBIOS) Project\nactivities and algorithm development. The document is organized into 6 separate volumes as\nOcean Optics Protocols for Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 4. Volume I: Introduction, Background and Conventions; Volume II: Instrument Specifications, Characterization and Calibration; Volume III: Radiometric Measurements and Data Analysis Methods; Volume IV: Inherent\nOptical Properties: Instruments, Characterization, Field Measurements and Data Analysis Protocols;\nVolume V: Biogeochemical and Bio-Optical Measurements and Data Analysis Methods; Volume VI:\nSpecial Topics in Ocean Optics Protocols and Appendices. The earlier version of Ocean Optics Protocols for Satellite Ocean Color Sensor Validation, Revision 3 (Mueller and Fargion 2002, Volumes 1\nand 2) is entirely superseded by the six volumes of Revision 4 listed above.\n\n14. SUBJECT TERMS\n\n15. NUMBER OF PAGES\n\nSIMBIOS, bio-optical data, radiometric data, ocean optics protocols.\n\n76\n\n16. PRICE CODE\n17.
